201 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Boyanovsky


SC No. S33380


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 131; 743 P.2d 711; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1778


January 20, 1987, Argued and submitted  
September 15, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. TM84-3967; CA No. A39414.


*  Appeal from District Court, Benton County, Henry R. Dickerson, Judge. 81 Or App 670, 728 P2d 868 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The decision of the trial court is reversed. The case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was stopped at a roadblock and arrested for driving under the influence of intoxicants under Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540. The State sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the trial court's suppression of evidence gathered in the roadblock, the primary purpose of which was to detect persons driving under the influence of intoxicants.


OVERVIEW: The primary purpose of the roadblock in which defendant was stopped was to detect persons driving under the influence of intoxicants and gather evidence for use in those persons' criminal prosecutions. The secondary purpose was to check driver licenses and vehicle registrations. The trial court had balanced the public need for sobriety roadblocks against the individual's interest in privacy to reach the conclusion that the evidence should not have been suppressed. The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals and reversed the trial court's decision. The court noted that before government officials could embark on a search or seizure for evidence to be used for this purpose, they must have individualized suspicion of wrongdoing. The court found that because the stopping of defendant's car and his seizure occurred in the absence of any belief that he had committed an offense, the officers failed to comply with the constitutional standards for searches and seizures provided by Or. Const. art. I, § 9.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals and reversed the trial court's decision not to suppress the evidence obtained against defendant.

 

202 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Nelson v. Lane County


SC Nos. S33066, S33082, S33115


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 97; 743 P.2d 692; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1780


October 8, 1986, Argued and submitted  
September 15, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 16-83-05689; CA No. A32607.


*  On appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County, Maurice K. Merten, Judge. 79 Or App 753, 720 P2d 1291 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed. The decision of the trial court is affirmed in part and reversed in part. The case is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver brought an action against defendants, county, state, and officers (collectively defendants), seeking injunctive relief and punitive damages under the Oregon Tort Claims Act (Act), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 30.260-.300, alleging violation of state law and state and federal constitutional rights. The trial court granted defendants summary judgment. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Police officers established a nighttime sobriety roadblock (a fixed checkpoint at which all cars were stopped). The driver was stopped, questioned about her alcohol consumption, detained for a sobriety field test and released. The court of appeals held that the roadblock violated the warrant clause of Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The court affirmed and held that roadblocks were seizures of persons. The power to seize did not arise by implication from Or. Rev. Stat. § 181.030, allowing the police broad authority to "prevent crime." Authority to conduct the roadblocks as an administrative search required a law or ordinance detailing the purpose of the search and the limits of executive authority. No such authority existed in the case sub judice. Thus, the driver was entitled to declaratory relief that defendants' conduct was illegal. The Act barred a punitive damages claim against defendants. Punitive damages were unavailable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 because no violation of the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, occurred. The officers' discretion was limited. All drivers were stopped. Only if there was suspicion a driver was intoxicated was a filed sobriety test conducted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the rulings of the court of appeals. The court remanded to the trial court allow the driver to develop the factual bases of her tort theories.

 

203 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cornell


SC No. S33887


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


304 Ore. 27; 741 P.2d 501; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1577


July 7, 1987, Argued and submitted  
August 24, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 85-1107, 85-1106; CA Nos. A38557, A39036.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court for Washington County, Honorable Alan C. Bonebrake, Judge. 83 Or App 559, 732 P2d 922 (1987).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed; the decision of the trial court is reversed. The cases are remanded to the trial court with instructions to reinstate the indictments and for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, the prosecution contested the decision of the Oregon Court of Appeals that affirmed the trial court judgment which dismissed defendants' indictments for aggravated murder and felony murder on the ground that the term "torture" as used in Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(1)(e) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: The lower courts found that the term "torture" as used in § 163.095(1)(e) was unconstitutionally vague. The prosecution appealed and prevailed. A criminal statute violated Or. Const. art. 1, §§ 20, 21 if it was so vague that it allowed a judge or jury unbridled discretion to decide what conduct was prohibited in a given case. A statute did not need to define an offense so precisely that, in every case, a person could determine the specific conduct that would fall within that statute's reach, but a reasonable degree of certainty was required by Or. Const. art. I, §§ 20, 21. Under the federal constitution, due process required that a statute defining criminal conduct be sufficiently explicit to provide notice of the forbidden conduct. Application of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(1)(e) required the prosecution to prove that defendant had the infliction of pain as one of the objectives for his conduct, apart from responsibility for the victim's death. The statute did not require the prosecution to prove that the pain was inflicted for one of several enumerated purposes such as revenge, coercion, or sadistic pleasure. Motive was rarely a criminal element.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court judgments and remanded the causes to the trial court for reinstatement of defendants' indictments and further proceedings consistent herewith.

 

204 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Loyer


SC No. S33447


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


303 Ore. 612; 740 P.2d 177; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1561


March 4, 1987, Argued and submitted  
August 4, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 85-136; CA No. A38155.


*  On appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County, Honorable Charles A. Sams, Judge. 81 Or App 445, 725 P2d 613 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the trial court and the Court of Appeals are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the decision of the trial court that defendant was not allowed to examine the psychiatrist at the sentencing hearing following his plea of guilty to assault in the third degree and attempted kidnapping in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: After defendant pleaded guilty to assault in the third degree and attempted kidnapping in the second degree, the trial judge ordered a psychiatric examination preparatory to considering sentencing under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.735, the dangerous offender statute. The State presented the written report of the examining psychiatrist who failed to appear. Defendant moved the court to reset the hearing and direct the witness to appear so that defendant would be able to examine the witness. The trial court denied the motion, and defendant was sentenced as a dangerous offender. He appealed, claiming error in the trial court's denial of his earlier request to withdraw his guilty plea and the court's failure to allow him to examine the psychiatrist. The Court of Appeals affirmed. Because defendant made no claim that the procedural flaw prevented the court from determining upon appellate review whether the sentence exceeded the maximum sentence allowable by law or was unconstitutionally cruel and unusual, he did not raise a challenge to the sentence itself and therefore, his claim of procedural error was beyond the court's limited scope of review.


OUTCOME: The decisions of the trial court and the court of appeals were affirmed.

 

205 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Oregonian Pub. Co. v. O'Leary


SC No. S33348


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


303 Ore. 297; 736 P.2d 173; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1346; 14 Media L. Rep. 1019


January 20, 1987, Argued and submitted  
April 28, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 85-1-108; CA No. A36591.


*  Appeal from Judgment of Clackamas County Circuit Court, Richard D. Barber, Judge. 80 Or App 754, 724 P2d 822 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The case is remanded to the Court of Appeals for consideration of the plaintiffs' remaining assignments of error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, newspaper and reporter, appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which declared that the closed hearing provision of Or. Rev. Stat. § 136.718 did not violate the Or. Const. art. I, § 10 and the U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV. Named as defendants were the judge, the state, and the county district attorney.


OVERVIEW:  Or. Rev. Stat. § 136.617 provided that in criminal proceedings, the prosecutor could move the court to order a witness to testify or produce evidence. A summary hearing, held outside the presence of the jury and the public, would then determine if the witness should be compelled to testify or produce evidence. The newspaper and reporter brought an action after the reporter and other members of the public were excluded from a summary hearing in a highly publicized murder trial. The circuit court granted defendants' motions to dismiss. The appellate court affirmed and held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 136.617 was constitutional because the public's right to access only encompassed the right to relevant and admissible evidence. On appeal, the court held that either the hearing must be open or the government must acquiesce in the witness' refusal to testify. The government could not create a secret court by pleading that it must act in secret to avoid infringing the witness' right against self-incrimination. Rather, both obligations must be satisfied to not violate the open administration of justice provision in the Or. Const. art. I, § 10.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and held that the closed hearing provision was unconstitutional.

 

206 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Dunn v. Redmond


SC No. S33474


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


303 Ore. 201; 735 P.2d 609; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1202


March 4, 1987, Argued and submitted  
April 14, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * LUBA No. 84-074; CA No. A40877.


*  Appeal from Judicial Review of a Final Order of the Land Use Board of Appeals. 82 Or App 36, 727 P2d 145 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to that court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which rejected respondent property owner's request for judicial review of a final order of the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA) after it rejected the property owner's claim that ordinances adopted by the city were invalid because the ordinances took property without just compensation and deprived the property owners of due process of law.


OVERVIEW: The property owner requested LUBA to review and invalidate Redmond, Wash., Ordinances 595-596, which regulated land use. The property owner did not seek to force a sale to the city or compensation for any taking that the city might have committed. LUBA rejected the property owners claims. When they sought judicial review the intermediate court decided on its own motion that the challenge went beyond LUBA's jurisdiction and ordered LUBA to dismiss the proceeding. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for further proceedings. The court held that LUBA had broad jurisdiction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.835(8) to consider the validity of the ordinances, and that LUBA had not lost jurisdiction just because a taking claim was asserted. The court noted that the property owner had not sought to compel compensation. Rather, he had challenged the land use ordinances, which fell within LUBA'S jurisdiction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.825, and its scope of review under § 197.835.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the intermediate court that ordered the dismissal of the property owner's administrative challenge to certain land use regulations and remanded for further proceedings.

 

207 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Lewis v. Oregon Beauty Supply Co.


Nos. TC 8206-03404, CA A32019, SC S32697, SC S32703


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 616; 733 P.2d 430; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1165; 2 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1135


Argued and submitted July 2, 1986  
February 24, 1987, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals * 


*  Appeal from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Honorable Clifford B. Olsen, Judge. 77 Or App 663, 724 P2d 618 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent employee filed an action for intentional infliction of severe emotional distress and intentional interference with an economic relationship against petitioners, company, owner, and owner's son. The Oregon Court of Appeals upheld the jury verdict and punitive damages award against the son on the interference with an economic relationship claim and reinstated the verdict and punitive damages award against the owner on the same claim.


OVERVIEW: The company, owner, and owner's son appealed the court of appeals' decision. On appeal, the court reversed in part and affirmed in part, reinstating the trial court's judgment. The court found that the son's conduct was intentional as required for the interference with contractual relationship claim. There was testimony that he told other employees that the employee would not be working for the company much longer. He also refused to cooperate with her when she required information in order to perform her job. The son knew that he was interfering with the employee's job performance and therefore had the requisite knowledge that he was interfering with her employment relationship. The son's conduct also was in pursuit of an improper motive. He physically and verbally intimidated the employee and also threatened her and defamed her to other employees. Those were improper means that in fact resulted in injury to the employee's employment relationship with the company. The court found that there was insufficient evidence of intentional conduct necessary to sustain a verdict against the owner or the company for interference with a contractual relationship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part the judgment entered against the son, reversed in part the judgment against the company and the owner, and reinstated the trial court's judgment.

 

208 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ray


Nos. CC B66-078, CA A34281, SC S32986


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 595; 733 P.2d 28; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 1134


Argued and submitted October 8, 1986  
February 10, 1987, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals.

Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court, Laurie K. Smith, Judge. 79 Or App 529, 719 P2d 922 (1986).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that reversed defendant's conviction under the telephone harassment statute in Or. Rev. Stat. § 160.065(1)(e).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with harassment under § 166.065(1)(e). Section 166.065(1)(e) prohibited subjecting another to alarm or annoyance by telephonic use of obscenities or description of sexual conduct. Defendant filed a demurrer to the complaint on the grounds that § 166.065(1)(e) was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad on its face. The district court overruled the demurrer, and defendant was convicted of the charge. The appellate court reversed the district court. The State sought review. The court affirmed, holding that § 166.065(1)(e) violated the right to freedom of speech provided in Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The court reasoned that § 166.065(1)(e) was overbroad because it would also prohibit calls between spouses, lovers or friends that may from time to time cause annoyance and consensual calls from legitimate polling organizations collecting data on attitudes relating to sexual matters. Further, the language of § 166.065(1)(e) proscribed legitimate calls from physicians to patients dealing with sexual matters that cause alarm. The court also concluded that § 166.065(1)(e) was vague because it unconstitutionally delegated power to a judge or jury to decide what was obscene.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment that reversed defendant's conviction under the telephone harassment statute.

 

209 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Henry


SC No. S32941


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 510; 732 P.2d 9; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 2242; 14 Media L. Rep. 1011


October 8, 1986, Argued and submitted  
January 21, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 31-300, 31-301; CA No. A26439.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court for Deschutes County, Honorable John Copenhaver, Judge. 78 Or App 392, 717 P2d 189 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed, but for the reasons stated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state of Oregon appealed from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which reversed defendant's convictions, holding that Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.087 was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant had been prosecuted and convicted of dissemination of obscene material and possession of obscene material with the intent to disseminate.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of dissemination of obscene material and possession of obscene material with the intent to disseminate in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.087. The appellate count reversed the convictions, holding that the statute was unconstitutionally vague and the State of Oregon sought review. It upheld the lower appellate court's decision reversing the convictions, on different grounds. Rather than finding the statute to be constitutionally vague, the court held that the statute violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8, because it restricted speech on a ground (obscenity) that could not be justified as an historical exception from Oregon's constitutional guarantee of freedom of expression. The court noted that Oregon's free speech protection, covering "any subject whatever," was broader than that of the U.S. Constitution as interpreted by the U.S. Supreme Court. The court reviewed the historical development of obscenity laws and concluded that restrictions on sexually explicit or obscene expressions were not well established at the time that its Constitution was adopted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, holding that the statute under which defendant was convicted was unconstitutional, but for different reasons.

211 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Oliver v. Crookham


No. S32447


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 533; 731 P.2d 1018; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 2244


May 6, 1986, Argued and submitted  
January 21, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus. No. C8411-34753.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ to issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, who was the defendant in a criminal case, brought a mandamus proceeding to challenge the decision of defendant, the then presiding judge of the Circuit Court for Multnomah County (Oregon), to deny relator's motion for a change of judge.


OVERVIEW: Relator filed a motion for a change of judge supported by an affidavit asserting that he could not receive a fair trial before the judge to whom the case had been assigned. The circuit court judge denied the relator's motion after a hearing on grounds that the relator had failed to show any actual prejudice, that the motion had been filed for tactical reasons and, in any event, that the judicial disqualification statutes were unconstitutional. Relator then brought a writ mandamus proceeding. The court held that the circuit court judge erred. The court held that the judicial disqualification statutes, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 14.250-14.270, did not violate the separation of powers or elections of judges clauses in the state constitution as set forth in Or. Const. art. III, § 1 and art. VII, § 1, respectively. The court also held that in counties having presiding judges, the presiding judge could call upon the moving party to show the reasons for the party's good faith belief.


OUTCOME: The court issued the peremptory writ in relator's mandamus proceeding to challenge the circuit court judge's decision to deny relator's motion for a change of judge.

 

212 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Bowman v. Crookham


No. S32524


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 544; 731 P.2d 1025; 1987 Ore. LEXIS 2247


May 6, 1986, Argued and submitted  
January 21, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus. No. C8501-34199.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ to issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator, a criminal defendant, petitioned for a writ of mandamus to compel defendant presiding judge to grant relator's motion under the Oregon judicial disqualification statutes, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 14.250-.270, for a change of judge in relator's criminal prosecution.


OVERVIEW: In her criminal prosecution, relator filed a motion for a change of judge on the ground that the assigned judge was prejudiced against her and her attorneys. The relator brought her mandamus action after the presiding judge orally informed her counsel that he would deny the motion because §§ 14.250-.270 were unconstitutional and declined to schedule a hearing on the motion, except before the assigned judge in relator's criminal case. The court granted the writ of mandamus, holding that §§ 14.250-.270 were constitutional. Relator was entitled to a hearing on her motion for a change of judge because her allegation that the assigned judge was prejudiced against criminal defendants was sufficient to disqualify the judge if it was made in good faith. The court also ruled that the hearing on the motion had to be heard by a judge other than the judge assigned to her criminal case. Allegations that the assigned judge was a former political opponent of relator's counsel and that he had a reputation for drinking were not found sufficient to disqualify the assigned judge because there were assaults on the judge as a person.


OUTCOME: The court granted relator's petition and issued a peremptory writ of mandamus.

Wilson v. Department of Revenue


SC No. S31577


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 128; 727 P.2d 614; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1773


October 2, 1985, Argued and submitted  
October 28, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Oregon Tax Court, Edward M. Howell, Judge Pro Tempore. 10 OTR 17 (1985). OTC No. 2106.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the Oregon Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought review of a judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which affirmed the assessment by defendant, the Oregon Department of Revenue (department), of additional personal income taxes for three taxable years.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers operated a partnership in Oregon. By condemnation, the United States acquired a parcel of real property owned by the partnership, which realized a gain for income tax purposes. The taxpayers sought to defer recognition of the gain for federal and state income tax purposes by acquiring like-kind property pursuant to 26 U.S.C.S. § 1033. The like-kind property was located in Oregon and Illinois. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.290(1), the department disallowed deferral for the portion of the gain invested in the Illinois property and assessed additional income taxes. The tax court affirmed the decision of the department. The court affirmed the judgment of the tax court, holding (1) Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.290 did not violate the equal protection clause of the Oregon Constitution, Or. Const. art. I, § 20, (2) Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.290 did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 32 because it uniformly applied to all similarly situated Oregon residents, and (3) Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.290 did not violate the Commerce Clause, U.S Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3, because it had neither a discriminatory purpose nor a discriminatory effect on interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the tax court, which affirmed the department's assessment of additional income taxes upon the taxpayers.

Oregon Education Asso. v. Phillips


SC No. S33296


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 87; 727 P.2d 602; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1768


October 7, 1986, Argued and submitted  
October 21, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 86-C-10367; CA No. A41468.


*  Appeal from Judgment of Marion County Circuit Court, Val D. Sloper, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of trial court modified as set forth herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, secretary of state and attorney general, challenged a judgment of the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon), which required the secretary to perform a one subject review of a ballot measure pursuant to Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d) in response to appellee challengers' constitutional challenge to the ballot measure.


OVERVIEW: The ballot measure was a proposed amendment to the Oregon Constitution affecting property taxes. The challengers sought summary judgment that the measure violated Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d) (one-subject rule) or that the trial court order the secretary to review the measure for compliance with that section. Appellants filed a motion for summary judgment on their affirmative defense of laches. The trial court ordered the secretary to perform the review. On appeal, the court held that the measure's change from a dollar limit to a percentage of value limit were connected to ad valorem tax limitation and the repeal of a part of the constitution and adoption of a new tax rate system in the same measure were properly connected with the ad valorem tax limitation. The court did not find that the trial court erred, but modified the trial court's decision to reflect the holding that the measure did not violate the one-subject rule.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's judgment upon finding that the ballot measure did not violate the Oregon Constitution's one-subject rule.

 

215 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Eckles v. Woolley


SC No. S31738


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


302 Ore. 37; 726 P.2d 918; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1762


July 9, 1985, Argued and submitted; February 7, 1986, Reassigned  
October 7, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. 16-83-00926; CA No. A28776.


**  Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County, Edwin E. Allen, Judge. State ex rel Eckles v. Livermore, 72 Or App 650, 696 P2d 1153 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals, reversing the trial court, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner business sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, which reversed the trial court's decision granting judgment for the business in its lawsuit against respondent directors of the new state accident insurance fund corporation (SAIF) and ordering SAIF to return to the form it had prior to the enactment of 1979 Or. Laws 829.


OVERVIEW: The business brought a lawsuit against the directors pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.510(3), seeking a judgment that SAIF was a nullity and non-existent. The court affirmed the appellate court's decision reversing the trial court's decision granting judgment for the business. SAIF was neither a private corporation nor a municipal corporation and was not within the prohibition under Or. Const. art. XI, § 2, which prohibited the formation of corporations by the legislative assembly's special laws. The Legislative Assembly expressly chose the form of a corporation to manage the workers' compensation insurance coverage. The legislature wanted to give the program numerous features common to insurance companies. However, SAIF was not a corporation as a form for conducting business enterprise with private investment by and for the profit of stockholders or a corporation as a form of organizing municipal authority and services with territorially-defined taxing authority. The creation of a corporation for a public purpose was permitted under Or. Const. art. XI, § 2, as long as the members were citizens and it was an instrument of the government subject to legislative control.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision reversing the trial court's decision granting judgment for the business in its lawsuit against the directors and ordering SAIF to return to its previous form.

 

216 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Farley


SC No. S32706


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 668; 725 P.2d 359; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1486


July 2, 1986, Argued and submitted  
September 16, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from Court of Appeals. * TC No. 84-1870-C; CA No. A35584.


*  Appeal from the Circuit Court for Josephine County, Gerald E. Neufeld, Judge. 78 Or App 102, 714 P2d 1079 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the judgment of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII) and driving with a suspended license (DWS), Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560. Defendant pled guilty to DUII, but the circuit court dismissed the felony DWS on the ground of former jeopardy. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW:  Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.515(2) barred separate prosecutions for offenses based on the same criminal episode. However, a guilty plea was no bar to subsequent prosecution under a charge filed no later than 30 days after entry of the plea, with the guilty plea voided at defendant's request, Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.525(2). The court reversed, holding that a plea of guilty was jeopardy, though the state later offered to vacate the conviction. Under Or. Const. art. I, § 12, statutory violations were the same offense for jeopardy purposes even if each contained different elements. A conviction of a guilty plea was similar to a conviction after trial or an acquittal, for jeopardy purposes. A second prosecution was for the same offense if the charges arose out of the act and were known to the state at the time of the first prosecution and could have been brought in the first prosecution. While driving while intoxicated and driving without a license were elements of separate statutes that defendant's single act of operating the automobile violated, defendant could not have been twice put in jeopardy for that same act in successive prosecutions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision which reversed the trial court's order dismissing the charge against defendant for driving with a suspended license on the ground of double jeopardy.

 

217 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Teledyne Wah Chang Albany v. Powell


No. S33132


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 590; 724 P.2d 319; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1479


August 20, 1986, Argued and submitted  
August 27, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On petition to review ballot measure explanatory statement.



DISPOSITION: Ballot Measure explanatory statement certified as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner waste facility sought review of the explanatory statement for Ballot Measure 15 prepared for inclusion in the voters' pamphlet for a November 4, 1986 election.


OVERVIEW: An explanatory statement was prepared to accompany Ballot Measure 15 in the voters' pamphlet. The waste facility contended that the explanatory statement was insufficient and unclear. Specifically, the explanatory statement failed to state that its sole immediate effect would have been to close down a waste facility operated by the waste facility and to require the removal of all radioactive deposits. The court noted that because the waste facility was the only facility affected by the measure, it was appropriate to identify it as so. The court held that the failure of the committee to state that effect rendered it insufficient. However, other wording recommend by the waste facility was repetitious. The court also concluded that the explanatory abatement adequately stated the measure's practical effect, which was to regulate described zirconium wastes not presently regulated by the Energy Facility Siting Council. The court also determined that the statement correctly stated that radioactive materials, which pose no significant danger to public health and safety, would not be regulated as radioactive waste by the Council.


OUTCOME: The court modified the explanatory statement which was to be included with the voters' pamphlet.

 

218 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. Eugene School Dist.


SC Nos. S32472, S32469


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 358; 723 P.2d 298; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1458; 53 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1012


April 1, 1986, Argued and submitted  
July 28, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. A31423.


*  Judicial review from Order of Verne A. Duncan, Superintendent of Public Instruction for the State of Oregon. 76 Or App 146, 708 Pd 1161 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, local school district and state school superintendent, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that reversed respondent teacher's suspension and revocation of her teaching certificate pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 342.650, 342.655 for wearing religious dress while teaching because it violated the First Amendment. The teacher's civil rights action was moot due to the previous restoration of her certificate.


OVERVIEW: Respondent teacher, a Sikh, was suspended under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 342.650 for wearing religious clothing while teaching and then had her teaching certificate revoked under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 342.655 by appellant local school district and appellant state school superintendent respectively. The appellate court reversed the superintendent's order, holding that it violated the teacher's First Amendment rights. The court held that the teacher's civil rights action was moot because her certificate had been returned, she had moved to another state, and the statutes were valid. The court held that the school district should not have been granted standing before the appellate court to file a petition that sought to affirm, not oppose, the superintendent's decision; that the statutes should not have been invalidated on federal Constitutional grounds because they were valid on state constitutional grounds; and that the superintendent could rule on the constitutionality of the statutes, not just enforce them. The court held that the statutes contributed to the children's religious rights and were a minimal restriction to insure the religious neutrality of the public schools.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court. The court held that appellant state school superintendent's revocation of respondent teacher's teaching certificate was valid under state constitutional law because the applicable statutes were a minimal restriction of the teacher's rights in order to insure the religious neutrality of the schools and facilitate public education.

 

219 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Oregon Education Asso. v. Roberts


SC No. S32734


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 228; 721 P.2d 833; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1447


June 4, 1986, Argued and submitted  
June 26, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 152580; CA No. A35391.


*  Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Richard D. Barber, Judge. 78 Or App 32, 714 P2d 1060 (1986).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to the circuit court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, association, union, and individuals, sought review of a judgment from the Marion County Circuit Court (Oregon), which affirmed a decision for defendants, Secretary of State (Secretary) and Attorney General, in an action brought to prevent the adoption of an initiative petition without a prior determination by the Secretary regarding the petition's compliance with the "one subject only" rule of Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d).


OVERVIEW: In bringing an action to compel the Secretary and the Attorney General not to process an initiative petition and to prevent the initiative petition's circulation, plaintiffs claimed that before voters could adopt the proposed measure, the Secretary was required to determine whether the initiative petition complied with the "one subject only" rule of Or. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d). When the appeals court upheld a judgment for the Secretary and the Attorney General, plaintiffs sought review, and the court reversed. Based on the plain meaning of the word "proposed" in art. IV, § 1(2)(d), the court held the Secretary's duty to certify compliance with art. IV, § 1(2)(d) arose before an election. Moreover, in determining at which stage in the process of submitting a proposed law to the voters the Secretary was required to certify compliance with art. IV, § 1(2)(d), the court held certification was required when the Secretary approved a prospective petition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment that affirmed a decision for the Secretary and the Attorney General in an action plaintiffs brought to prevent the adoption of an initiative petition without a prior determination by the Secretary regarding the petition's compliance with the "one subject only" rule of the Oregon Constitution.

 

220 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Lake Oswego v. Mylander


SC No. S32578


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 178; 721 P.2d 433; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1153


May 7, 1986, Argued and submitted  
June 3, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. 85-202; CA No. A36290.


*  Petition for review of dismissal by the Court of Appeals of appeal of judgment of the Clackamas County Circuit Court, Dale Jacobs, Judge. 77 Or App 310, 713 P2d 44 (1986).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded to the Court of Appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The City appealed a decision in favor of defendant by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which suppressed evidence against defendant in a prosecution for driving while under the influence of intoxicants in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540 (repealed).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving while under the influence of intoxicants and was cited to appear in municipal court. The municipal court granted defendant's motion to suppress. The City appealed to the circuit court, which ruled against the City. The City then appealed to the court of appeals. Defendant claimed that the City was not entitled to two consecutive appeals where the constitutionality of the statute was not in question. The court held that the circuit court and the court of appeals had appellate jurisdiction. The circuit court had jurisdiction to review the municipal court's decision pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 157.010, 157.020, as it was when defendant was charged. The court of appeals had appellate jurisdiction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 153.565(3) because the City was merely prosecuting the traffic violation for the State. Although the City was not entitled to two appeals for a violation of the City's ordinance or charter, the violation was of a state traffic law. Therefore, because under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.060(3) the State had the right to appeal an order suppressing evidence, the City also had the right to appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision in favor of defendant, ruled in favor of the City, and remanded the cause to the court of appeals.

Hay v. Or. Dep't of Transp.


SC No. S32179


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


301 Ore. 129; 719 P.2d 860; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1145


March 12, 1986, Argued and submitted  
May 20, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** CC No. 83-394; CA Nos. A32892, A32904.


**  Original petition for judicial review of administrative rule, 75 Or App 119, 705 P2d 249 (1985); appeal from Clatsop County Circuit Court, Thomas E. Edison, Judge, 75 Or App 125, 705 P2d 242 (1985).



DISPOSITION: On the appeal (CA A32892), the Court of Appeals is affirmed; the petition for judicial review (CA A32904) is dismissed as moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirming the trial court's judgment, which granted appellee agencies' joint motion for partial summary judgment on liability and for judgment on the pleadings in the property owners' suit alleging trespass and nuisance, and upholding the validity of Or. Admin. R. 736-24-005(2)(d)(A).


OVERVIEW: The owners challenged the agencies' statutory authorization for the use of the beach in front of their motel as a parking area. Three issues were presented: (1) whether the court's review of a court of appeals decision that upheld the validity of a rule was rendered moot when an agency promulgated a new rule superseding the challenged rule; (2) whether a circuit court determined the validity of a rule in the course of a civil action for nuisance and trespass when the claims arose from a state agency's actions pursuant to a rule; and (3) if the circuit court could determine a rule's validity, whether the rule at issue in fact valid, thereby justifying the agencies' motion for judgment on the pleadings. The court dismissed the petition for judicial review because the challenged rule had expired and was superceded by a new rule. The court held that only when a party placed a rule's validity at issue in a separate civil action could circuit courts determine a rule's validity. The court found that the general policy of Or. Rev. Stat. § 390.610(4) was to do whatever was necessary to preserve and protect scenic and recreational use of Oregon's ocean shore.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the petition for judicial review and affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals.

Rogers v. Roberts

300 Ore. 687; 717 P.2d 620; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1132

April 1, 1986

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner proponents sought review of the ballot title for their proposed constitutional amendment that the attorney general certified to respondent Secretary of State.


OVERVIEW: The proponents filed a prospective petition to initiate a constitutional amendment with the secretary. The attorney general submitted a draft ballot title for the proposed amendment to the secretary and, following the proponent's comments, certified to the secretary the revised ballot title. The proponents sought review of the ballot title. The court concluded that the revised ballot title submitted by the attorney general did not substantially comply with the requirements of Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035 because it did not correctly identify the subject matter or accurately state the chief purpose of the proposed measure. The use of the term "banning" in the caption of the revised ballot title did not accurately identify the subject matter because the measure did not actually ban or prohibit any property use regulation. The measure prohibited government taking of property value or rights through zoning that was not essential to public health and safety without the payment of just compensation. The government's cessation of such zoning because of the increased cost was speculation, which was improperly included as a consequence of the measure. The court certified a proper ballot title.


OUTCOME: The court refused to certify the ballot title for the proponent's constitutional amendment that the attorney general certified to the Secretary of State.

 

223 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. De Mello


SC No. S32077


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 590; 716 P.2d 732; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1128


January 29, 1986, Argued and submitted  
March 18, 1986 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * DC No. M337553; CA No. A32628.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County District Court, Stephen L. Gallagher, Judge. 74 Or App 503, 703 P2d 276 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction for driving while his license was suspended under Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560. Defendant argued that he should be acquitted because he was not notified of his suspension as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.570, and, even if notice had been sent, the State's case proved his affirmative defense that he had not received notice of suspension.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving while his license was suspended. He argued for acquittal because the Motor Vehicles Division of the Department of Transportation did not notify him of his suspension as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.570, and, even if the division did send the notice, the State's case proved his affirmative defense that he had not actually received notice of suspension. The court affirmed the conviction. The Division had sent a letter notifying defendant of the suspension by certified mail, return receipt requested. Defendant did not claim the certified mail, and it was returned to the Division marked "unclaimed." The court held that defendant had been properly notified under § 482.570 and that the statute did not require actual receipt. Defendant could not establish the affirmative defense of lack of notice by not claiming certified mail after he was advised by the postal service that the certified mail was to be picked up at the post office. The legislature did not intend to create a loophole for suspended drivers by allowing them to avoid receipt of notice of suspension simply by refusing or failing to claim certified mail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for driving while his license was suspended.

 

224 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Portland v. Dollarhide


SC Nos. S31408, S31398


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 490; 714 P.2d 220; 1986 Ore. LEXIS 1122


May 8, 1985, Argued and submitted  
February 4, 1986 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied March 18, 1986.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * DA No. 261430; CA No. A31427.


*  Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County, Frank L. Bearden, Presiding Judge, 71 Or App 289, 692 P2d 162 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: City and defendant both sought review from the Oregon Court of Appeals, which agreed with the trial court that the city's ordinance prohibiting prostitution was valid, and that the city's mandatory minimum penalty provision was invalid. Unlike the trial court, the court of appeals found the invalid penalty provision severable and remanded for trial.


OVERVIEW: The city had adopted an ordinance that defined and prohibited prostitution in terms substantially identical to the state and also adopted a mandatory minimum penalty provision for violation of the ordinance. There was no mandatory minimum penalty for a conviction of prostitution under state law. Defendant was charged with prostitution under the ordinance. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss, claiming state criminal law "preempted" the ordinance. The district court granted the motion. The city appealed. The court of appeals agreed with the trial court that the city's mandatory minimum penalty provision was invalid, but that the ordinance prohibiting prostitution was valid. Both defendant and the city petitioned for further review. On review, the Oregon Supreme Court affirmed. The city's mandatory minimum penalty for prostitution was harsher than the state's minimum penalty for the same offense. Thus, the court held the city's mandatory minimum penalty provision, as applied to the crime of prostitution was invalid. The court concluded, as did the court of appeals, that the invalid mandatory minimum penalty section was severable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that held that the city's prostitution ordinance was valid and severable from the invalid penalty provision. The court affirmed the court of appeals' reversal and remand for trial.

Davidson v. Or. Gov't Ethics Comm'n


SC No. S32001


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 415; 712 P.2d 87; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1692


November 6, 1985, Argued and submitted  
December 24, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. A31304.


*  Judicial review from the Oregon Government Ethics Commission. 74 Or App 160, 702 P2d 417 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public official appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which upheld a decision of respondent Oregon Government Ethics Commission (commission) that the public official used his official position to obtain financial gain for himself in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 244.040(1) when he ordered a car as an "add-on" to the fleet purchase of his office and reimbursed his office for the price that it paid for the car.


OVERVIEW: As a vice president of a state accident insurance fund, the public official ordered the car after the general counsel of the fund opined that there would be no ethical violation. The commission found that the public official violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 244.040(1) because he obtained a savings he would not have realized but for his position as an official of the fund and ordered him to pay a forfeiture penalty. The public official argued that the commission had not proved the three elements of § 244.040(1) that (1) a public official (2) used his office (3) to cause or realize a financial gain. He argued that while he did realize a financial gain when he purchased the car, he did not "use" his office in doing so, and that (1) the statute prohibited only influence peddling and (2) the use of the public office and the benefit received must be causally connected. The court held that the commission's interpretation of the statute was compatible with the legislative policy to safeguard the public trust and that but for his position the public official would have been unable to purchase the car and, thus, to obtain a personal financial gain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, which upheld the commission's decision that the public official used his official position to obtain financial gain for himself.

 

226 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dykast


SC No. S31757


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 368; 712 P.2d 79; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1688


September 10, 1985, Argued and submitted  
December 17, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC Nos. 8301-0185, 8301-0186, 8301-0187; CA Nos. A30963, A30964, A30965.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Linn County. William O. Lewis, Judge. 73 Or App 98, 697 P2d 578 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which held that his right to a speedy trial under the Oregon and United States Constitutions was not violated where 560 days elapsed between his original arrest and trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on March 30, 1972. A trial was scheduled for August 23, 1982. The prosecution filed continuance motions. The motions were denied and both indictments were dismissed without prejudice. On January 25, 1983, defendant was re-indicted. His eventual trial date was October 12, 1983. Defendant claimed that his constitutional rights to a speedy trial were violated. The trial court convicted defendant. The court of appeals rejected defendant's speedy trial claim. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals. The court held that the 13 1/2 month delay, between August 23, 1982 and October 12, 1983, was attributable to the prosecution. The court could not say that the length of time was so long as to compel a finding that defendant's Or. Const. art. I, § 10 speedy trial right was violated. Further, while the reason for delay rested solely on the prosecution, its conduct was negligent, not intentional or malicious. Moreover, defendant suffered no prejudice in defending the charges made against him. Thus, on balance, defendant was not deprived of his art. I, § 10 speedy trial rights. The same results obtained under U.S. Const. amends. VI and XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

 

227 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Reynaga v. Northwest Farm Bureau


SC No. S31955


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 255; 709 P.2d 1071; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1681


October 1, 1985, Argued and submitted  
November 26, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * WCB No. 82-10833; CA No. A31941.


*  On appeal from Workers' Compensation Board Order date May 18, 1984. 74 Or App 151, 704 P2d 552 (1985).



DISPOSITION: Decision reversed and remanded to the Workers' Compensation Board for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant employee appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the denial by respondent insurer of payment for treatment by a California chiropractor. The employee had suffered a compensable injury to his shoulder, neck and back and was awarded permanent partial disability.


OVERVIEW: The employee, a migrant worker who worked in several different states, had suffered a compensable injury for which he was being treated in California by a chiropractor. The insurer began denying payment for treatment by the out-of-state chiropractor, although it indicated that it would pay for care by an orthopedist. The court reversed the decision affirming the denial of payment and remanded the case for further proceedings. Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.245 did not permit a compensation insurer to deny payment for treatment by all out-of-state chiropractors. The language of § 656.245 did not compel the interpretation that an insurer could exclude an entire category of health service providers located outside Oregon while paying for the same health service when provided in Oregon. The court held that a compensation insurer could not deny an entire category of otherwise reasonable out-of-state medical services. Thus, the insurer could not deny payment for treatment by all out-of-state chiropractors if that treatment, as specified in § 656.245, was for conditions resulting from the injury as the nature of the injury or the process of recovery required.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision and remanded the case to the Workers' Compensation Board for further proceedings.

 

228 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miller


SC No. S30798


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 203; 709 P.2d 225; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1575


October 8, 1984, Argued and submitted  
November 5, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. C82-08-36632; CA No. A27112.


*  Appeal from judgment of Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Robert E. Jones, Judge. 67 Or App 637, 680 P2d 676 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction for first degree manslaughter pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.118. At trial defendant had contended that the psychotherapist-patient privilege under Or. Evid. Code 504 protected statements that he had made to a psychotherapist and her secretary receptionist.


OVERVIEW: After killing his victim, defendant called his brother who told him to call a mental hospital. Defendant then called a state hospital and asked to speak with a doctor. The secretary asked him what the problem was and he stated that he had just killed a man. The psychiatrist who took the call talked with defendant while the secretary contacted the police who then picked defendant up at a pay phone. The court found that defendant specifically requested, and was assured of, confidentiality. The court found that the psychiatrist spoke to defendant in the same way she would have in a psychiatric interview. The statements were protected by the psychotherapist-patient privilege, Or. R. Evid. 504. The court also found the statements made to the secretary to be protected. However, the court found that the error in admitting the testimony of the psychiatrist and the secretary was harmless because there was substantial and convincing evidence of defendant's guilt and very little likelihood that the evidential error affected the trial court's decision. Under the inevitable discovery doctrine, the taint of the fruit of the illegal search of defendant's hotel room was removed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court.

Wright v. KECH-TV


SC No. S31616


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


300 Ore. 139; 707 P.2d 1232; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1572


September 3, 1985, Argued and submitted  
October 15, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied November 26, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 133,264; CA No. A28750.


*  Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Richard D. Barber, Judge. 71 Or App 662, 694 P2d 545 (1985).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, landowners, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the dismissal of their complaint by the circuit court in an action to compel removal of respondent television station's (station) transmission tower and related facilities from land designated for exclusive farm use.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court noted that thrust of the landowners' complaint was that the issuance of the permits violated the statewide land use planning goals, raising issues lying exclusively within the jurisdiction of the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA). Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 197.015(10), LUBA had exclusive jurisdiction to review "land use decisions." The initial inquiry on review was whether the issuance of the permits by the county was a land use decision. The court answered affirmatively. As such, a notice of intent to appeal the decision was required to have been filed with LUBA no later than 30 days after the date the decision sought to be reviewed became final. The landowners failed to do so and their appeal to LUBA was time-barred. The court rejected the landowners' argument that the redefinition of "land use decision" to exclude ministerial-type decisions was intended to clarify what the legislature had intended all along. It also rejected their' arguments regarding the constitutionality of LUBA. The court held that the doctrine of separation of powers did not prevent the exercise of adjudicatory functions by LUBA. The judgment below was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's affirmance of the dismissal of the landowners' complaint.

231 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Moyle


SC No. S30468


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 691; 705 P.2d 740; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1553


September 4, 1984, Argued and submitted  
August 27, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * DA No. 240844-8208; CA No. A28286.


*  Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County, Frank L. Bearden, Judge. 66 Or App 274, 673 P2d 1366 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed. The order of dismissal of the District Court is set aside and the case is remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with making two telephonic threats to inflict serious physical injury in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(d). Defendant filed a demurrer challenging the constitutionality of the statute. The trial court ruled the statute unconstitutional as violative of Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The Oregon Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's decision. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with making two telephonic threats to inflict serious physical injury, in that he threatened to kill one victim and her family and to bomb her home and car, and he threatened to kill another victim and to kidnap, rape and kill that victim's children. Defendant contended that Or. Stat. Ann. § 166.065(1)(d) violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8, and that it was impermissibly vague. The trial court found the statute unconstitutional because it focused on speech alone. The appellate court reversed, ruling that the statute was neither unconstitutional on its face nor as applied to defendant. The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment, but for different reasons. The court held that the specific phrases and terms in § 166.065(1)(d) to which defendant objected were not impermissibly vague in violation of the Oregon Constitution, because they adequately informed potential defendants of the prohibited conduct and they did not delegate uncontrolled discretion to a judge or jury to punish or withhold punishment. The court concluded that the statute also passed muster under the First Amendment of the U.S. Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, which found that the trial court erred in sustaining defendant's demurrer.

 

232 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Campbell


SC No. S30758


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 633; 705 P.2d 694; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1389


March 7, 1985, Argued and submitted; August 14, 1985, Reassigned  
August 20, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC Nos. 10-81-02891, 10-81-02892; CA No. A26455.


* Appeal from Circuit Court for Lane County. Edwin E. Allen, Judge. 68 Or App 53, 696 P2d 23 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case remanded to the trial court for proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a ruling by the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that permitted the admission into evidence of a statement made by the infant victim, but introduced by the victim's mother at trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of sexual abuse. Defendant contended that admission of a complaint made by the infant victim to her mother constituted inadmissible hearsay because the victim's mother introduced the statement at trial. The court determined that there was an exception to the hearsay rule in regard to the admission of complaints of sexual assault. The court determined that it was not for the parties involved to determine whether the child was incompetent as a witness and therefore unavailable, but for the judge below to have determined. The court noted that if competent, the victim's statement would have come in by her own testimony, and if not, the statement would have come in as a complaint of sexual misconduct under Or. Evid. Code 803(18a). The court also found that the statement was a relevant corroboration of defendant's confession. The court reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the prior ruling and remanded with instructions that the lower court determine whether the victim was competent as a witness.

Salem v. Bruner


SC No. S30627


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 262; 702 P.2d 70; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1296


July 11, 1984, Argued and submitted; March 26, 1985, Reassigned  
June 18, 1985 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied July 30, 1985.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 139,465; CA No. A28085.


*  Appeal from an Order Allowing Motion to Dismiss of the Court of Appeals dated March 15, 1984.



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed on different grounds. The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that dismissed his appeal of a conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants. The appeals court dismissed the matter citing lack of jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicants in violation of Salem, Or. Rev. Code § 100.330(a)(2) in the municipal court. The conviction was affirmed in the county circuit court and defendant sought review of that judgment in the court of appeals. The court of appeals dismissed the case for lack of jurisdiction under Or. Rev. Stat. § 221.360, which set forth a the dual appellate scheme for convictions under state statutes that were incorporated into the municipal code. The court, considering defendant's petition for review of the dismissal, held that in order to comply with the privileges and immunities clause of Or. Const. art. 1, § 20, the police officers should have relied upon meaningful criteria consistently applied to similarly situated defendants, rather than exercise "unbridled discretion" when they determined the appeal route by deciding whether to charge defendant with a violation of city ordinance or state statute and decided the court in which the trial was to be held. However, the court affirmed the dismissal of the appeal because defendant failed to argue, at the trial phase, that discretion was exercised in a standardless manner.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment dismissing defendant's appeal of a conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants.

Twentieth Century-Fox Film Corp. v. Department of Revenue


SC No. S30701


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 220; 700 P.2d 1035; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1288


December 13, 1984, Argued and submitted  
June 4, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * TC No. 1987.


*  Appeal from Decree of the Oregon Tax Court. Samuel B. Stewart, Judge. 9 OTR 407 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Tax Court is reversed and remanded with instructions to remand to the Department of Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Oregon Department of Revenue (department) appealed from a decree of the Oregon Tax Court, which held in favor of plaintiff taxpayer that the department did not prove that the statutory three-factor apportionment formula used to apportion income to Oregon for purposes of corporate excise taxation did not fairly represent the extent of the taxpayer's business activity in Oregon, thus the department improperly employed a different method.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer's tangible properties which entered Oregon were prints made from the negatives of taxpayer's motion pictures. Its business activity in Oregon was the distribution for display of the embodiment of a story or theme, photographed, edited, acted and captured on film. The taxpayer filed Oregon corporate excise tax returns using the statutory three-factor formula for apportionment of income to Oregon. The department modified the formula and determined that the taxpayer owed approximately $ 22,000 more. The taxpayer appealed to the Tax Court, which held that the department had not met its burden of proof to establish that the statutory apportionment formula did not fairly represent the extent of taxpayer's business activity in Oregon. The department appealed, and the court reversed. The court held that: (1) the department could modify the statutory formula; (2) Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.670 was applicable to remedy unconstitutional apportionment results and to provide for alternatives to statutory allocation and apportionment in unusual cases; and (3) Or. Admin. R. 150-314.670-(A) was valid because it was not inconsistent with Or. Rev. Stat. § 314.670.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision with instructions to remand to the department. The department proved that the apportionment provisions did not fairly represent the extent of taxpayer's business activity in Oregon and the department's modified apportionment formula was reasonable.

 

235 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hart


SC No. S31362


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 128; 699 P.2d 1113; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1239


March 7, 1985, Argued and submitted  
May 7, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 83-05-32325; CA No. A31581.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, Robert P. Jones, Judge. 70 Or App 731, 690 P2d 533 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's decision convicting defendant of assault and ordering him to pay restitution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of second degree assault and ordered to pay restitution after assaulting his 18 month old son and causing severe physical and mental injuries. The court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that defendant was not entitled to a jury for the restitution hearing. While defendant was entitled to have all the elements of a crime decided by a jury, the monetary cost of the victim's injury was neither an element of nor an act in the commission of the crime of assault in the second degree. Restitution was not intended to be the equivalent of a civil award and was an aspect of criminal law, not a quasi-civil recovery device. Therefore, defendant was not entitled to a civil jury trial on the issue of restitution. The restitution order did not violate the prohibition of excessive fines under Or. Const. art. I, § 16. Restitution orders were not denominated as fines and could be imposed in addition to fines. Moreover, the order was not excessive because the amount of the order had a reasonable relationship to defendant's criminal conduct since it was based on the actual injuries to his victim.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision convicting defendant of assault and ordering him to pay restitution.

State v. House


SC No. S30562


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 78; 698 P.2d 951; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1163


September 4, 1984, Argued and submitted; April 17, 1985, Reassigned  
April 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CC No. 82-0265; CA No. A25766.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Washington County, Donald C. Ashmanskas, Judge. 66 Or App 953, 676 P2d 892), aff'd as modified 68 Or App 360, 681 P2d 176 (1984).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed for the reason stated in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.062(1), read in conjunction with Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.060(10), restrained expression protected by Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and was overbroad. Defendant had been convicted of engaging in sexual conduct in a live public show, a violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.062(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with engaging in sexual conduct in a live public show. He unsuccessfully demurred to the indictment, challenging that Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.062(1) and Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.060(10) were unconstitutional under Or. Const. art. I, § 8 and the First Amendment. He appealed his conviction. The court of appeals held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.062(1) and Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.060(10) were unconstitutional, and the State appealed. In affirming, the court held that it was unnecessary to reach the constitutional question because as a factual matter defendant was entitled to a judgment of acquittal. The State was required to prove not only that defendant knowingly engaged in the described touching under § 167.060(10) but that he knowingly participated in the touching in an act of apparent sexual stimulation or gratification. The court found not one scintilla of evidence that defendant ever engaged in an act of apparent sexual stimulation or gratification. Consequently, defendant's motion for judgment of acquittal on the grounds that the state had produced no evidence on the material elements of the offense should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The court declined to reach the constitutional question but affirmed the court of appeals' judgment on another basis.

Burt v. Blumenauer


SC No. S30238


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


299 Ore. 55; 699 P.2d 168; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 1165; 51 A.L.R.4th 679


May 1, 1984, Argued and submitted  
April 23, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. A8112-07355; CA No. A25112.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Alan F. Davis, Judge. 65 Or App 399, 672 P2d 51 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant public officials sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversing the trial court's judgment in favor of the public officials. The issue was whether the public officials could have been held personally liable for the return of public monies expended to promote water fluoridation during an election period when that issue was before the voters.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayers filed suit against the public officials for unlawful expenditure of federal and matching county grant monies. The taxpayers contended that the public officials expended money for a purpose different than authorized by law when they spent on a fluoridation project at a time when an anti-fluoridation measure was on the city ballot. The trial court ruled in favor of the public officials and the appellate court reversed. On further review, the court affirmed the decision from the appellate court and remanded the case for further proceedings. Health officials did more than attempt to motivate individuals to take measures conducive to preserving their personal health. The public officials attempted to direct government behavior by soliciting citizens to vote to preserve a particular government policy. Whatever authority county health officials had to promote health practices did not extend to requiring public employees to oppose a measure during an election. Issues of fact remained with regard to whether the public officials required public employees to engage in these activities, and if so, whether the public officials had any defenses against liability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision from the appellate court that had reversed the ruling in favor of the public officials and remanded for further proceedings the case brought by the taxpayers alleging unlawful expenditure of federal and matching county grant monies.

Salem College & Academy, Inc. v. Employment Div.


SC No. 29528


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


298 Ore. 471; 695 P.2d 25; 1985 Ore. LEXIS 933


October 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
January 15, 1985 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. 80-T-56; CA No. A20465.


*  On judicial review from Judgment of the Employment Division dated March 5, 1981. 61 Or App 616, 659 P2d 415 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. Employment Division's decision is reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, employment division and associated individuals, sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1) and the Federal Unemployment Tax Act, 26 U.S.C.S. § 3309(b)(1), which excluded from covered employment services for religious organizations, were unconstitutional as an establishment of religion forbidden by U.S. Const. amend. I, in favor of petitioner employer.


OVERVIEW: The employer initiated action against the employment division and associated individuals contending that Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1) and the Federal Unemployment Tax Act (FUTA), 26 U.S.C.S. § 3309(b)(1), which excluded from covered employment services for religious organizations, were unconstitutional and in violation of U.S. Const. amend. I. The lower court held that the provisions were unconstitutional, and the employment division and associated individuals sought review. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 657.072(1)(a), employment was not inclusive of service performed in the employ of a church or association of churches. The court held that FUTA had not prevented a state from extending coverage of its unemployment insurance laws beyond federal requirements. However, the state was not allowed to violate the state constitution, Or. Const. art. I, §§ 2-5, in order to qualify for a benefit that was optional in the federal statute. The court held that the statutes were not in violation of the First Amendment if they extended to all schools, without regard to their religious affiliation. The court reversed a judgment of the lower court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment of the trial court, which held that Federal Unemployment Tax Act (FUTA) and state statute challenged by the employer, excluding religious organizations from covered employment services, were in violation of the First Amendment. The court granted judgment in favor of the employment division and associated individuals and held that the statutes were not unconstitutional if they extended to all schools.
