State v. Delgado


SC No. S31059


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


298 Ore. 395; 692 P.2d 610; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1934; 47 A.L.R.4th 643


November 6, 1984, Argued and submitted  
December 28, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. CR83-946; CA No. A30962.


*  Appeal from judgment of Polk County District Court, Walter W. Foster, Judge. 69 Or App 254, 684 P2d 630 (1984).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged an order from the Court of Appeals of Oregon, which reversed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of unlawfully possessing and carrying a switch-blade knife in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510, finding that the trial court erred in overruling defendant's demurrer and denying defendant's motion for acquittal on the grounds that § 166.510(1) was unconstitutional under Or. Const. art. I, § 27.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found to have a switch-blade knife in his back pocket by police. Defendant argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) making the mere possession and mere carrying of a switch-blade knife unlawful violated his right to bear arms under Or. Const. art. I, § 27. On appeal, the court determined that Or. Const. art. I, § 27 was intended to give private citizens the right to possess certain arms for the defense of person and property. The court ruled that the meaning of "arms" in the Oregon Constitution, as contemplated by the framers, included not only fire-arms, but also hand-carried weapons used for personal protection. The court concluded that a switch-blade was not so substantially different from its historical antecedent, the pocketknife, that it was included within the constitutional grant of a right to bear arms. The court held that because Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) proscribed the mere possession and mere carrying of the weapon it was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court's judgment.

Winn v. Gilroy


SC No. 29330 (Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 718; 681 P.2d 776; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1247


June 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
April 17, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied May 22, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 120015, 120016; CA Nos. A23680, A23681.


*  Appeal from Marion County Circuit Court, Val D. Sloper, Judge. 61 Or App 243, 656 P2d 386 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. Remanded to circuit court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother filed wrongful death actions against respondent father for the deaths of their children, who were killed in an accident resulting from the father's allegedly intoxicated and negligent driving of an automobile. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed the circuit court's dismissal of the complaint on the ground that the father was immune from liability for negligence toward his children. The mother sought review of the decision.


OVERVIEW: Two minor children died in an automobile collision while traveling in an automobile driven by their father. The children's mother, their personal representative, filed wrongful death actions against both drivers, alleging that each was responsible for the collision by one or more of several specifications of negligent driving, as well as wilful driving while intoxicated. The circuit court dismissed the complaints against the father on the ground that he was immune from liability for negligence toward his children, and the court of appeals affirmed the decision. On appeal, the court reversed the decision because the father was not immune from liability. The court held that the father's acts or omissions were not privileged and were tortious toward the children; therefore, the mother had stated a claim for relief. The court rejected the doctrine of parental immunity given the significant change in the state's policy concerning a driver's liability toward guest passengers.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals decision dismissing a mother's cause of action against her husband for the wrongful death of their children, which occurred in an automobile accident.

State v. Garcias


SC Nos. 29504, 29844, 29843


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 688; 679 P.2d 1354; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1208


November 3, 1983, Argued and submitted  
April 3, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA Nos. A26238, A26286, A26387, A26285, A26283, A26284, A27666, A27924, A27979, A27262, A27263.


*  Appeal from Lane County District Court. Laurie K. Smith, Judge; Bryan T. Hodges, Judge; and Frank R. Anderson, Judge. 62 Or App 472, 661 P2d 123 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decision is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the judgment of the district court that dismissed charges against defendants for violations of the menacing statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.190(1), on the grounds that the menacing statute was unconstitutionally overbroad.


OVERVIEW: After the district court had dismissed the charges against defendants for violations of the menacing statute, the state filed an appeal that argued the statute was not unconstitutional. The appellate court reversed, and defendants appealed. The court affirmed, holding that the statute did not violate Or. Const. art. I, § 8, nor was it unconstitutional as being overbroad and vague. The court examined whether the law was intended to limit communication, or whether its prohibition was directed against causing a harm that might be brought about incidentally by communication, and concluded that the latter applied. No privilege applied to speech that was intended to place another in fear of imminent serious physical injury. Further, after interpreting the menacing statute in the context of its common law assault origins, it did not reach communications protected either by the constitution or common law privilege. Finally, although the statute did not reach constitutionally protected behavior and an overbreadth analysis did not apply, the court found that the statute adequately informed citizens, judges, juries, and prosecutors of the conduct it made criminal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that reversed the judgment of the district court dismissing charges against defendants for violations of the menacing statute on the grounds that it was unconstitutionally overbroad.

De Fazio v. Washington Public Power Supply System


SC No. 29649


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 550; 679 P.2d 1316; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1174


June 9, 1983, Argued and submitted  
March 20, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied May 1, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. George C. Woodrich, Judge. Certification from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 16-81-11344; CA No. A26721.


*  On certification from the Court of Appeals by order dated May 24, 1983.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the circuit court is reversed and the case remanded to that court for entry of a judgment and such further proceedings as may be necessary.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, utility, cities, and utility boards (participants), appealed the order of the Circuit Court (Oregon), which following the challenge of plaintiff ratepayers and cities, voided their agreement to assign future utility revenues under the Thermal Power Facilities Act (TPFA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 225.450 - 225.490, 261.235 - 261.255, the Joint Operating Agency Act, Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 262.005-262.115, and Or. Const. art. XI, § 9.


OVERVIEW: The participants agreed to pay part of their future utility revenues to guarantee construction funds for the utility's nuclear power plant. The project failed. The ratepayers filed suit to have the agreements nullified. After interventions on both sides, the trial court found that the agreements ultra vires under the charters and Or. Const. art. XI, § 9. The participants appealed. The court reversed. Legislative history was not evidence of the prior law. The Joint Operating Act and the TPFA were supplemental grants of authority to the charters and not restrictions. Prior voter approval was not needed because the utility issued the bonds, and not the cities. The debt limitation laws had a "special fund" exception which allowed the pledge of future utility revenues. Section 9 was not violated because the participants were not direct or indirect stockholders in the utility. The § 9 prohibition on loans of credit applied only to the exposure of the government's taxing power, and not other revenue sources such as the utility revenues. The cities and the utility boards had the authority to enter into the agreement, which was unrelated to the success or failure of the project.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the circuit court for the ratepayers, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

State v. Florea


SC No. 29870


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 500; 677 P.2d 698; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1108


December 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
March 6, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 39577; CA No. A25448.


*  On appeal from Benton County Circuit Court, Hon. Frank Knight, Judge. 63 Or App 871, 667 P2d 583 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction for theft and official misconduct under ORS 162.415(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a former chief of police, was convicted by a jury of theft and of official misconduct. He asserted that the statutory definition of official misconduct was unconstitutionally vague. Defendant pointed out that the indictment referred to the unlawful exercise of his official duties, while the statute used the phrase "in his official duties." The court held that the use of the word "in" meant to forbid knowing performance of an act that constituted an unauthorized exercise of the powers or opportunities of one's official position. Defendant argued that because the statute applied to a great variety of public servants whose duties were not fixed by any law, written job description, or other document, judges and juries were not able to refer to any fixed standard. The court held that what was "unauthorized" was often a conclusion of law on which a judge was to instruct the jury. The statute did not leave a judge or, under proper instructions, a jury with unconstrained discretion to define a crime. The court held that if there was vagueness, it did not lie in ORS 164.415(1)(b).


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner nonprofit corporation challenged a ballot title prepared by the Attorney General and certified to respondent Secretary of State for a proposed initiative that would amend the Oregon Constitution.


OVERVIEW: For a proposed amendment to the Oregon Constitution, the Attorney General prepared the following ballot title: CONSTITUTIONAL HOMESTEAD EXEMPTION INCOME TAX FUNDED, LOCAL GOVERNMENT SPENDING LIMIT. A nonprofit corporation petitioned for review of the ballot title, proposing the following: CONSTITUTIONAL HOMESTEAD EXEMPTION AND RENTER EQUIVALENT, LOCAL GOVERNMENT SPENDING LIMITS. Basically, the nonprofit corporation argued that the ballot title should include a reference to a "renter equivalent" to the homestead exemption. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.085, the court's mandate was to review the proposed ballot title to determine whether it was insufficient, not concise or unfair. The nonprofit corporation's failure-to-include argument was not well suited for resolution under § 250.085. Thus, where reasonable minds could differ, the court would follow the judgment of the Attorney General for he had the responsibility to make the choice in the first instance. However, the court agreed with the nonprofit corporation. Under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 250.035, 250.039, the court certified the following ballot title: INCOME TAX FUNDED HOMESTEAD EXEMPTION/RENTER EQUIVALENT, LOCAL SPENDING LIMITATIONS.


OUTCOME: The court granted the nonprofit corporation's petition to revise a ballot title.

State v. Turner


SC No. S30048


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 451; 676 P.2d 873; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1059


January 26, 1984, Argued and submitted  
February 15, 1984 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for Rehearing Denied March 27, 1984.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. C82-06-35699, CA No. A27352.


*  Appeal from Multnomah County Circuit Court, J. C. Beatty, Judge. 64 Or App 634, 669 P2d 842 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which approved his sentences for the offenses of burglary in the first degree, kidnapping in the first degree, attempted rape in the first degree and assault in the fourth degree. Defendant was sentenced to 30 years as a dangerous offender under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.725 and received a minimum sentence of 15 years pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted and sentenced on three felonies and a misdemeanor. The trial court imposed a minimum sentence for each crime.Defendant was also found to be a dangerous offender and received a maximum 30 year sentence under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.025. The trial court imposed a 15 year minimum sentence (half of the 30 year indeterminate sentence), on the dangerous offender finding, pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 144.110. Defendant appealed, arguing that the minimum sentence provision could not be applied to the dangerous offender sentence. The court affirmed the sentence, holding that the minimum sentence was lawful, and that none of the minimum sentences violated the proportionality requirement of state and federal law. Defendant had argued, based on case law, that because no minimum sentence could be applied to murder, a minimum sentence for any lessor offense would be disproportionate. The court disagreed, holding that prior case law did not say that no minimum could be imposed for murder, but rather, that the legislature had not imposed a minimum sentence for murder. The court could not find disproportionality when comparing minimum sentences for different, unrelated crimes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, approving defendant's sentences.

 

248 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Department of Revenue v. Carpet Warehouse, Inc.


SC No. 28984


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 400; 676 P.2d 299; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1052


December 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
February 8, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * TC No. 1611.


*  Appeal from order of contempt issued on October 2, 1982, nunc pro tunc August 27, 1982, Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Oregon Tax Court issued alternative writs of mandamus directing defendant officer, defendant warehouse, and defendant contractor to file tax returns or show cause why they should not. When they failed to comply with the alternative writs, the tax court issued preemptory writs of mandamus. Subsequently, the tax court issued judgment finding the officer in contempt for failing to obey the peremptory writs.


OVERVIEW: In the alternative writs, the officer was directed to file individual income tax returns for several years. The corporations were also directed to file corporate excise tax returns for multiple years. In response to the subsequent preemptory writs, the officer and the corporations filed tax return forms providing their names, addresses, and telephone numbers, but no other information. The remainder of the forms asserted the privilege against self-incrimination. Following a hearing, the officer was found in contempt of court and ordered to be incarcerated until he complied with the peremptory writ. On appeal, the officer and the corporations asserted the preemptory writs should not have issued. The court affirmed in part because the officer and the corporations brought the situation upon themselves by not filing returns, precluding them from arguing a self-induced inability to comply as a defense, and they filed the functional equivalent of no tax return, precluding application of the privilege against self-incrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the issuance of the preemptory writs and the judgment finding the officer in contempt.

 

249 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Hart v. Paulus


SC No. S30242


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 352; 676 P.2d 1384; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1032


January 26, 1984, Argued and submitted  
February 1, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Petition for Declaratory Judgment.



DISPOSITION: Declaratory Judgment rendered that procedures required by SJR 30, Par. 3, do not comply with Or Const Art XVII, § 1. Petitioners' requested order that respondent place proposed constitutional amendments on an election ballot is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, property owners and taxpayers, filed an action against respondent secretary of state seeking a judgment declaring and adjudicating S.J. Res. 30, para. 3, 1st Spec. Sess. (Or. 1983) to be illegal and not in compliance with Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1, and directing the secretary of state to place three proposed constitutional amendments on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: S.J. Res. 30, 1st Spec. Sess. (Or. 1983) provided that three constitutional amendments would be referred to the voters. Paragraph 3 of the resolution provided that the resolution would not be effective unless local government units representing at least a majority of the population of the state and a majority of such units ratified the amendment and requested its placement on the ballot. The court held that paragraph 3 violated Or. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The court held that § 1 required the legislature itself to reach agreement on the desirability of submitting proposed constitutional amendments to the people. The court held that no statute or joint resolution could add a requirement that a proposed amendment be requested or ratified by someone else. The court also held that there was a justiciable controversy between the parties.


OUTCOME: The court declared that the procedures required by the resolution did not comply with the state constitution and denied petitioners' request that the secretary of state be directed to place the proposed constitutional amendments on an election ballot.

State v. Pottle


SC Nos. 29615, 29616


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


296 Ore. 274; 677 P.2d 1; 1984 Ore. LEXIS 1030


October 5, 1983, Argued and submitted  
January 24, 1984 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 20-567B, CA No. A22439.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Washington County. Gregory E. Milnes, Judge. 62 Or App 545, 662 P2d 351 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress wiretap evidence on the ground of facial invalidity of the wiretap order under Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.724(5).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murder and appealed the conviction to the lower court alleging, inter alia, that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress wiretap evidence on the basis that the wiretap order was defective in failing to include minimization, termination, and immediate execution provisions required by § 133.724(5). The lower court reversed the denial and remanded for a new trial. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment holding that the order was fatally defective under § 133.724(5). The court determined that the order did not mention that the interception be executed as soon as practicable. The court found that the order did not address the minimization of the interception of non-pertinent conversations, in that it did not inform officers to conduct the wiretap in such a way as to minimize interception of such calls. The court also found that the state did not set out a precise objective in the application for the order and that although the order contained a duration of 15 days, it failed to instruct officers that if they gained the objective authorized in the order in some lesser period than contained therein, the interception had to cease.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that reversed the denial of defendant's motion to suppress wiretap evidence on the basis that the wiretap order was fatally defective under state statutory law.

The ballot that was approved by the court also explained to the voters that it was incomplete and incorporated as many of the changes as possible.


OUTCOME: The ballot title was certified, as modified, by the court.

Teledyne Wah Chang Albany v. Paulus

Rev. Stat. § 469.375. Pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.035 and Or. Rev. Stat. § 250.065, respondent attorney general drafted a ballot title.


OVERVIEW: The ballot title provided: Adds Requirements for Approval of Radioactive Waste Disposal Sites. The question posed was: should the Council be required to consider additional factors before approving a site for the disposal of radioactive wastes. The explanation contained in the ballot title provided that the measure would add to existing requirements by requiring the Council to find, before approving a site for the disposal of radioactive wastes, that the site was not subject to water erosion, earthquakes, volcanoes, or landslides; that there was no safer choice for such disposal; and that there would be no radioactive release from the waste site. The litigant argued that the failure to include the phrase "naturally occurring radioactive wastes" would mislead voters by suggesting that the new requirements applied to controversial types of radioactive wastes. The court modified the ballot title by providing in the title: Adds Requirements for Disposing Wastes Containing Naturally Occurring Radioactive Isotopes. The court added the language "naturally occurring radioactive isotopes" in the question and explanation sections of the ballot title.


OUTCOME: The court certified the ballot title as modified.

 

253 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Macy


SC No. 29558


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 738; 671 P.2d 92; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1604


August 1, 1983, Argued and submitted  
October 18, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied December 6, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. 27992, CA No. A26229.


**  Appeal from Polk County Circuit Court, H. W. Devlin, Judge. 62 Or App 477, 661 P2d 586 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is reversed and the sentence is partially vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Polk County Circuit Court (Oregon), which convicted him of two counts of murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment with a 10-year mandatory minimum on one count imposed under ORS 144.110(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of murder. Following the conviction, the trial court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment with a 10-year mandatory minimum on one count imposed under ORS 144.110(1), which stated that in a felony case, a trial court could impose a minimum term of imprisonment of up to one-half of the imposed sentence. Thereafter, defendant sought review, claiming that the trial court had no authority to impose a mandatory minimum under ORS 114.110(1) because the statute did not authorize a minimum term in the case of a life sentence. In reversing and vacating that portion of the sentence imposing a 10-year minimum, the court held that the legislature had not enacted a provision for imposing a minimum term for murder convictions. Because the legislature never intended to impose a minimum term for life imprisonment and because ORS 144.110(1) did not authorize a sentencing judge to speculate what the exact life span of any given individual would be, the court held that ORS 144.110(1) was not applicable to a life sentence.


OUTCOME: In reversing, the court vacated that portion of the sentence imposing a 10-year minimum.

 

254 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Nearing v. Weaver


SC No. 29424


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 702; 670 P.2d 137; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1549


July 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
October 4, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. 26761; CA No. A22918.


**  Appeal from Columbia County Circuit Court, Thomas Edison, Judge. 61 Or App 297, 656 P2d 965 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. Remanded to circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner stalking victims appealed the order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a grant of summary judgment to respondents city and police officers for failure to follow the mandatory arrest provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.310(3) for violation of a domestic protective order. The trial court held that the defenses of official discretion and official immunity precluded potential liability for psychic and physical injuries.


OVERVIEW: A violent husband stalked his estranged family and friends, assaulted them, and made threats despite a protective order filed with the city police department. Police officers failed to arrest him despite the mandatory arrest provisions of Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.310(3). The stalking victims filed a complaint that sounded in negligence for the failure to follow the statute. On appeal, the court held that although a separate claim might be pleaded for negligence, the claim here was for violation of a statutory duty and that negligence in the technical sense was not involved. The court held that Oregon law did allow claims for emotional injury arising from the breach of a statutory duty. The court held that the statute was intended to protect a specific class of beneficiaries and that police officers were mandated to arrest as soon as there was probable cause to believe that one person has assaulted another or placed the other in fear of imminent serious physical injury. The court held that although the officers exercised judgment in evaluating complaints, they had no true discretion as to whether to arrest, although factual or legal defenses might have been available for inaction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court that had affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the city and its police officers. The court held that it was possible for the stalking victims to recover for either psychic and emotional injuries or physical injuries that were caused by the police officers' failures to comply with a mandatory arrest statute and remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

McNutt v. State


SC No. 28619


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 580; 668 P.2d 1201; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1442


July 15, 1982, Argued and submitted  
September 7, 1983

** Appeal from Lane County Circuit Court. George Woodrich, Judge. 56 Or App 545, 642 P2d 692 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed a judgment finding that a sentence imposed on petitioner for violation of ORS 541.615(1) was void. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pled guilty to violating ORS 541.615(1), which prohibited anyone from remove any material from the beds or banks or fill any state waters without a permit issued under authority of the Director of the Division of State Lands. The charge averred that petitioner unlawfully and with criminal negligence failed to stop work in a stream when directed to do so. Petitioner argued in his action for post-conviction relief that the trial court erred in treating the conviction as a misdemeanor because the statute was outside the Oregon Criminal Code and required no culpable mental state. The intermediate court held that the conviction was a misdemeanor, and the court affirmed. The court found that the statutes facially made it clear that the removal of material in violation of ORS 541.615(1) was a misdemeanor. While the statute required no culpable mental state, it did not clearly dispense with a culpable mental state requirement. The court found that a prosecutor could therefore upgrade a violation of ORS 541.615(1) to a misdemeanor by alleging and proving a culpable mental state.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

256 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Circuit Court of Oregon, Fifteenth Judicial Dist. v. AFSCME Local 502-A


SC No. 29403


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 542; 669 P.2d 314; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1502; 115 L.R.R.M. 2179


June 7, 1983, Argued and submitted  
August 31, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. A23894.


*  Judicial Review from Employment Relations Board. (No. C-65-81) 61 Or App 311, 657 P2d 1237 (1983).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, county board of commissioners, county juvenile department and a juvenile court judge, challenged a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed an order from the Employment Relations Board (board) requiring the judge to bargain collectively with respondent union, the exclusive representative of county juvenile court counselors.


OVERVIEW: The board applied the Public Employee Collective Bargaining Act (PECBA), Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 243.650-.782, and ordered the judge to bargain collectively with the union regarding the terms of the counselors' employment. Petitioners sought review, challenging the constitutionality of PECBA as applied. The court of appeals affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The employment relationships between petitioners and the counselors were subject to PECBA. The board had performed its administrative function in ensuring petitioners administered their employment relationships in compliance with state laws and the applicable collective bargaining agreement. It was not performing a judicial function or the employment function delegated by Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.604(1) to the juvenile court. The application of PECBA caused no interference of unconstitutional proportion. PECBA did not divest the juvenile court of authority to make personnel decisions, nor did the mandate to bargain collectively hinder the juvenile court in the resolution of cases. The juvenile courts' ability to adjudicate cases was in no way reduced by the obligation to bargain with counselors concerning employment conditions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that the judge was required to bargain with the union regarding the terms of the juvenile court's employment of the county juvenile court counselors.

Portland v. Tuttle

August 16, 1983

*  Appeal from Multnomah County District Court, Linda L. Bergman, Judge. 62 Or App 62, 659 P2d 1010 (1983).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's denial of her motion for a jury trial on a charge of interfering with the police in violation of Portland City Code § 14.08.140(b) in favor of respondent city.


OVERVIEW: The question before the court was whether a violation of Portland City Code § 14.08.140(b) constituted a crime requiring the city to prove defendant acted with a culpable mental state and triggered defendant's right to a jury trial under Or. Const. art. I, § 1 and Or. Rev. Stat. § 136.001. The court answered the question in the affirmative, reversed the denial of defendant's motion for a jury trial, and remanded. The court found that the culpable mental state of "knowledge" was required by the language of the ordinance and explicit in the complaint. The court reasoned that in order for defendant to "unlawfully refuse" to heed the order of a police officer, the defendant must knowingly refuse to obey the lawful order of the officer. The court also found that Portland City Code § 14.08.020's penalty coupled with the explicit and implicit culpable mental states indicated an intent by the Portland City Council to create a crime and not a violation or infraction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of defendant's motion for a jury trial and remanded for trial.

 

259 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lowry


SC No. 28989


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 337; 667 P.2d 996; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1367


April 8, 1983, Argued and submitted  
July 26, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. 79-1588-C; CA No. A22852.


*  Appeal from Josephine County Circuit Court. Gerald C. Neufeld, Judge. 59 Or App 338, 650 P2d 1062 (1982).


State v. Lowry, 59 Ore. App. 338, 650 P.2d 1062, 1982 Ore. App. LEXIS 3211 (1982)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. Remanded to circuit court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which convicted him of unlawful possession of a controlled substance under Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992.


OVERVIEW: After stopping defendant's car for a faulty headlight, the deputy arrested him for driving under the influence of intoxicants. While defendant was handcuffed, another officer took a small pill bottle containing white powder from defendant's clothing. The officer kept the bottle and a test of it contents revealed that it was cocaine. Defendant was convicted of unlawful possession over his objections to the warrantless search and seizure. On appeal, the court reversed, and held that the evidence should have been suppressed. The court determined that seizure of the pill bottle occurred during the course of an arrest for a crime unrelated to the bottle. The court found that a warrantless seizure made incident to a lawful arrest had to be limited to items that reasonably related to the probable cause for the arrest. The court ruled that other effects unrelated to the reason for the arrest could also have been seized if their nature as contraband was evident on sight. The court concluded that where a test of unknown substance was necessary, the officer was required to secure the item for the least amount of time needed to obtain a warrant for further search.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment convicting defendant of unlawful possession.

 

260 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Freeland


SC No. 28856


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 367; 667 P.2d 509; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1370; 44 A.L.R.4th 371


November 1, 1982, Argued and submitted; June 8, 1983, Reargued and resubmitted  
July 26, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. C81-04-32282; CA No. A22721.


**  58 Or App 163, 647 P2d 966 (1982). On appeal from Circuit Court of Multnomah County. William M. Dale, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. The decision of the circuit court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged by information with robbery in the second degree. A preliminary hearing was scheduled, but an indictment was obtained instead. Defendant nevertheless requested a preliminary hearing, which was denied. Defendant filed a motion for preliminary hearing or dismissal of the indictment in circuit court. The circuit court dismissed the indictment. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The circuit court had found, and defendant contended on appeal, that because the decision whether to proceed by information after preliminary hearing, or by indictment, rested within the discretion of the prosecutor, the procedure violated the requirement of Or. Const. art. I, § 20 that laws not grant any class of citizens privileges or immunities not belonging equally to all. The court agreed with the circuit court that even without ad hominem discrimination, the case fell within the principle that equal treatment must not be denied "haphazardly" by ad hoc decisions that did not uniformly rest on meaningful criteria that made the privileges of a preliminary hearing equally available to all persons similarly situated, or, in the constitutional phrase, upon the same terms. The court stated that it was clear that such day-by-day "logistical" circumstances as the readiness of the prosecutor or the coincidence of an empty courtroom or a crowded hearing calendar could not determine the grant or denial of an important procedural step in the defense against a criminal prosecution, if the assurance that privileges had to be provided upon the same terms was to have any meaning.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and affirmed the decision of the circuit court.

State ex rel. Adult & Family Servs. Div. v. Bradley


SC No. 28949


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 216; 666 P.2d 249; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1341


February 8, 1983, Argued and submitted  
June 29, 1983 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. D 8008 66894, CA No. A22419.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Elvin Tarlow, Judge. 58 Or App 663, 650 P2d 91 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decision is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant alleged father appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the circuit court decision that Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.135(3), the six year statute of limitations then in effect for the filiation proceeding brought by plaintiff State of Oregon on behalf of the child for whom it was providing support, violated the federal right to equal protection of law and Or. Const. art. I, § 20.


OVERVIEW: The State brought a filiation action pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.125, for a child it had been supporting. The child was over seven years old when the State filed the action, and the alleged father pled the statutory limitation of six years. The trial court held that the "illegitimate" child's right to support from the natural parent was a substantial right and was a right equal to that of the "legitimate" child's. Hence, any statute limiting this right during a child's minority worked an illogical injustice on a child born out of wedlock and was constitutionally impermissible. The court of appeals ruled that weighing the state's interest in enacting the statutory limitation of six years against the constitutional rights of an illegitimate child to the protection of the laws equal to that afforded legitimate children, it hold that the six-year statute of limitations was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals and the circuit court because Or. Const. art. I, § 20 and the federal equal protection of law provisions did not allow total preclusion of the rights of illegitimate children.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions of the trial court and court of appeals.

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 227; 666 P.2d 802; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1342

June 29, 1983

**  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Clifford B. Olsen, Judge. 58 Or App 388, 650 P2d 1097 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The decisions of the trial court and the Court of Appeals are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court of Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him for possession of a controlled substance and being an ex-convict in possession of a firearm. Defendant challenged the warrantless entry and search of his motel room by police officers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for possession of a controlled substance and being an ex-convict in possession of a firearm. He sought to suppress evidence that was obtained during a warrantless entry and search of his motel room by police officers, and the search of his person after his arrest. The court was concerned with the validity of two searches and seizures, that of the motel room, which disclosed a gun, and that of defendant's person, which uncovered drugs. The court reversed because the police officers' warrantless entry into the motel room was, at most, based upon a reasonable suspicion, but supported with less than probable cause. A remote possibility of harm to the police officers did not justify a warrantless entry. Absent articulable facts that evidenced a compelling and urgent need for the entry, the state constitution demanded the issuance of a warrant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the defendant's conviction for possession of a controlled substance and being an ex-convict in possession of a firearm.

Southern Pacific Transp. Co. v. Department of Revenue


SC Nos. 28807, 28808, 28809, 28810 (consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


295 Ore. 47; 664 P.2d 401; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1238


February 10, 1983, Argued and submitted  
May 24, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied July 19, 1983.
On remand at Southern Pacific Transp. Co. v. Department of Revenue, 10 Or. Tax 80, 1985 Ore. Tax LEXIS 63 (1985)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Oregon Tax Court. * Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. OTC Nos. 1093, 1189, 1282, 1362.


*  Opinion of Oregon Tax Court dated February 22, 1982, and Decrees dated June 18, 1982.


S. Pac. Transp. Co. v. Dep't of Revenue, 9 Or. Tax 481, 1982 Ore. Tax LEXIS 25 (1982)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Oregon Tax Court is reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Oregon Department of Revenue sought review of the judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which held that appellee railroad utility's property did not include an out-of-state affiliate for purposes of Oregon's statute for assessing the property of designated utilities, which permitted valuation of the entire property of one's business, both within and without the state, as a unit.


OVERVIEW: Oregon's statute for assessing the property of designated utilities permitted the valuation of the entire property of a business, both within and without the state, as a unit. The railroad utility operated in Oregon and seven other states. The court below determined that the railroad utility's property did not include an out-of-state affiliate for purposes of the Oregon statute. The Oregon Department of Revenue challenged the lower court's decision. On appeal, the court determined that the railroad utility's property, for purposes of Oregon's statute for assessing the property of designated utilities, included the properties of the railroad utility's out-of-state affiliate. The court's decision was based on its finding that the affiliate's railroad operations were the railroad utility's property for purposes of Or. Rev. Stat. § 308.510(1). Accordingly, the lower court's judgment was reversed and remanded.


OUTCOME: The lower court's holding that the railroad utility's property did not include an out-of-state affiliate for purposes of Oregon's statute for assessing the property of designated utilities, was reversed and remanded.

 

264 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Sanok v. Grimes


SC No. 28554


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 684; 662 P.2d 693; 1983 Ore. LEXIS 1154


December 6, 1983, Argued and submitted  
April 19, 1983 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 7, 1983.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from order of the Oregon Tax Court. Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. OTC No. 1736.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded to Oregon Tax Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a decision of the Oregon Tax Court that denied his motion to join the Department of Revenue (Department) as a necessary party and dismissed his action against respondent county assessor and three county appraisers.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer brought an amended complaint in the tax court, naming as defendants the county assessor and three county appraisers. The defendants filed a motion to dismiss on grounds that they were not proper parties and that the Department was a necessary party not joined. The taxpayer argued that jurisdiction was proper in the tax court because all of his claims arose under tax law, and he filed a motion to join the Department if it was a necessary party. The tax court denied the motion to join and dismissed the complaint. The taxpayer appealed. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that that some of the claims of the complaint did "arise under" tax law and were within the tax court's jurisdiction. The court found that a claim was not one "arising under the tax laws" unless it had some bearing on tax liability. Therefore, the court opined that the taxpayer's tort claims against tax assessors were not within the subject-matter jurisdiction of the tax court. However, the court concluded that the claims regarding the taxpayer's qualification for forest-land tax status did "arise under" the tax laws and were within the tax court's jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the taxpayer's suit qualification for forest-land tax status and remanded, but affirmed the dismissal of the taxpayer's tort claims.

Bernstein Bros., Inc. v. Department of Revenue

Ross v. Springfield School Dist.


SC No. 28590


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 357; 657 P.2d 188; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1342


July 15, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 30, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * CA No. 19038.


*  Appeal from Fair Dismissal Appeals Board. 56 Or App 197, 641 P2d 600 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals decision is reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which upheld a decision by the Fair Dismissal Appeals Board (FDAB) that found the facts relied upon by the respondent school board in its dismissal of the teacher to be true and supported by the statutory grounds of immorality and gross unfitness.


OVERVIEW: The board dismissed a permanent teacher on grounds of inefficiency, immorality, and gross unfitness after the teacher was found at an adult bookstore during an undercover investigation by the police department. The teacher appealed to the FDAB, which affirmed the dismissal. The appellate court upheld the FDAB's decision. The teacher appealed and the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the case. The court found that the commission had conducted a preliminary investigation and found a lack of probable grounds for action against the teacher under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 342.175-342.177. The court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 342.865(1)(i) did not confer on the school board or the FDAB independent authority to interpret the grounds for revocation of a teaching certificate. Where the commission had determined that a particular set of facts did not constitute grounds for revocation neither the school board nor the FDAB could reach a different interpretation. The court held that the order affirming the teacher's dismissal was inadequate. The FDAB nowhere set forth any basis upon which it could have concluded that the teacher's conduct violated that standard.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment that upheld the FDAB's decision that dismissed a teacher on the statutory grounds of immorality and gross unfitness. The court remanded to the FDAB for a determination of whether the facts as to immorality were adequate to justify the statutory grounds.

State v. Mai


SC No. 28146


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 269; 656 P.2d 315; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1332


February 10, 1982, Argued; July 23, 1982, Reargued  
December 29, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. 19767.


*  Appeal from District Court, Clackamas County. Robert D. Burns, Judge. 54 Or App 334, 634 P2d 1367 (1981).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the appellate court (Oregon), which affirmed his conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540 and evading a police officer in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.555. The trial court allegedly violated defendant's right to compulsory process under Or. Const. art. I, § 11, and U.S. Const. amend. VI.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's attorney refused to comply with the reciprocal discovery statutes, claiming that his client had an absolute right to call witnesses, a right guaranteed by the compulsory process clause of the state and federal constitutions. The district court imposed a sanction permitted by Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.865 and refused to permit a witness to testify. The court affirmed defendant's conviction. The court found that the preclusion sanction of § 135.865 was not inconsistent with Or. Const. art. I, § 11, provided that the district court found that the prosecution was prejudiced by defendant's failure to comply with the reciprocal discovery statutes, and provided further that it appeared that no sanction short of preclusion effectively would avoid the prejudice, which defendant's lack of compliance created. Although the preclusion sanction procedures under the discovery statutes were permissible, the statute had to be applied in a reasonable manner by imposing the sanction, which would infringe least upon defendant's rights and which would achieve the goal of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

Suess Builders Co. v. Beaverton


SC No. 28615


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 254; 656 P.2d 306; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1334


October 5, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 19, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * CA No. A20101, TC No. 40569.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court of Washington County, Holger M. Pihl, Judge. 56 Or App 573, 642 P2d 361 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed. Remanded to circuit court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Oregon) granted appellee city's motion to dismiss appellant property owner's complaint, in which it was claimed that the city deprived the landowner of the rental value of his property by designating the land as a future park and that this constituted a compensable taking under state and federal law. The property owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The property owner alleged that the city temporarily deprived it of the rental value of the property at issue and caused a permanent depression of its market value by designating the major part of the property as a future park site in the city's comprehensive land use plan. The property owner claimed this constituted a compensable taking of property under Or. Const. art. I, sec. 18 and the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments. The complaint also alleged claims under 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 1983 and 1985(3). On appeal from the trial court's dismissal, the court found that the complaint sufficed to survive a motion to dismiss. According to the complaint, the city told the property owners to hold parts of their land for the park district, subject to taxes and without an opportunity to make economic use of it or to place it on the market, until the district was politically and financially ready to buy it for the planned park. This language was broad enough to state a claim. The § 1985(3) claim was property dismissed absent some "discrimination," allegation but the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim was also reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's ruling and remanded the action to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

269 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Cole v. State


SC No. 28687


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 188; 655 P.2d 171; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1319


November 1, 1982, Argued and submitted  
December 15, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Oregon Tax Court. * Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. TC Nos. 1778, 1779, 1780.


*  Order of Oregon Tax Court (May 5, 1982) Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Oregon Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a decision from the Oregon Tax Court, which dismissed their action against defendant, the Oregon Department of Revenue, challenging the Department's assessment of personal income taxes and interest against the taxpayers.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers failed to pay their personal income taxes. The Department assessed the taxpayers. The taxpayers initiated an action, claiming that the assessment was improper. The trial court dismissed the action, finding that the taxpayers failed to pay the assessments prior to judicial review as required by 1982 Or. Laws ch. 29. The taxpayers claimed that to require payment of the challenged assessment prior to judicial review deprived them of property without due process of law. The taxpayers also alleged equal protection violations in that the requirement of payment before judicial review applied to income taxes, but not to other taxes. In affirming the decision, the court found that the taxpayers' equal protection rights were not violated. Every taxpayer that appealed an assessment of a personal income tax had to pay the tax before judicial review. The taxpayers were not treated differently than any other appealing taxpayer. The collection of the tax prior to judicial review was not a deprivation of property without due process. The taxpayers had opportunities to challenge the assessment through an informal administrative hearing and a formal quasi-judicial hearing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision.

Lewis v. Department of Revenue


SC No. 28121


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 139; 653 P.2d 1265; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1317


July 15, 1982, Argued and submitted  
November 30, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the judgment of the Oregon Tax Court. Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. OTC No. 1413.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the state department of revenue, sought review of that part of a judgment of the Oregon Tax Court that granted attorney's fees to respondent taxpayer on the basis of the tax court's inherent equitable powers. The tax court further declared unconstitutional Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.250(4), which granted an exemption from property tax to widows of war veterans. The tax court also denied the taxpayer's requested relief of an exemption.


OVERVIEW: The department denied the taxpayer, a widower of a war veteran, an exemption from property tax under § 307.250(4). The tax court found that the gender-based exemption was unconstitutional, and the result was that no person, a widow or widower, would be entitled to the exemption. The taxpayer sought fees under Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.495, which was applicable to judicial review of certain agency actions. The tax court properly found that provision inapplicable to the tax court. However, the court found that the tax court erred in granting fees based upon inherent powers. There was no right to fees by a statute or by contract. Further, the department was not put on notice that it could be found liable for fees. The taxpayer's complaint did not act to vindicate the rights or equities of a large class of similarly situated persons. Additionally, the taxpayer was not a prevailing party where the tax court did not grant the requested relief of an exemption.


OUTCOME: The court modified the tax court's judgment by deleting an award of attorney's fees and otherwise affirmed the decree.

Benton County v. Friends of Benton County

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed an order that had been entered by the Land Use Board of Appeals (LUBA). Laws 772, § 4 and that the county had erred in granting a conditional use permit to expand a gravel extraction operation.

Hanson v. Or. Dep't of Revenue

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers, founders of a family trust, sought review of an order of summary judgment issued by the Oregon Tax Court, which held that their family trust was ineffective to transfer income to the trust and that the taxpayers owed additional individual income tax.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers formed a family trust to cause their income from personal services and income-producing property to be income of the trust, rather than individual income. Taxpayers filed a complaint. The taxpayers' trial counsel appeared on another matter and counsel interpreted remarks of the judge as prejudicial. Her motion to disqualify the judge was denied. As to collateral estoppel, the court found that tax court correctly stated that both courts considered the issue of whether the taxpayers were taxable or whether the trust was taxable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that the family trust was ineffective to transfer income to the trust and that the taxpayers owed additional individual income tax.

 

273 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Hewitt v. State Accident Ins. Fund Corp. (In re Williams)


SC No. 28252


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


294 Ore. 33; 653 P.2d 970; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1291


April 7, 1982, Argued and submitted  
November 16, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. ** CA No. 19548.


** Judicial Review from Workers' Compensation Board (No. 79-7248) 54 Or App 398, 635 P2d 384 (1981).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, state workers' compensation fund (state fund), challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which was in favor of respondent claimant. The state fund argued that the claimant was not due benefits under Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.226 because § 656.226 did not violate either the Fourteenth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution of Or. Const. art I, § 20.


OVERVIEW: The claimant cohabited with and had a child with a female who was killed in an industrial accident that was compensable under the state workers' compensation system. The claimant applied for benefits under Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.226 for himself and the child. The state fund denied benefits to the claimant because § 656.226 covered only female survivors of covered workers. The claimant challenged, and the court of appeals reversed. The state fund appealed. The court affirmed. The court held that § 656.226 violated both the Fourteenth Amendment to the U.S. Constitution and Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The classifications made in § 656.226 were inherently suspect because they were made on the basis of gender. Section § 656.226 did not overcome this suspicion because the classification did not reflect specific biological differences between men and women. It was based on stereotypes. Therefore, § 656.226 was the kind of classification that could not withstand constitutional challenge. However, the court did not invalidate § 656.226 because to do so would be to deny the claimant and his child benefits. Accordingly, the court affirmed that § 656.226 extended benefits to the claimant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal by the appellate court of the decision of the state fund. The claimant was entitled to benefits under the state workers' compensation system.

 

274 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Caraher


SC No. 28360


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 741; 653 P.2d 942; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1190


March 3, 1982, Argued and submitted; September 8, 1982, Reargued  
November 2, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied December 7, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from Court of Appeals. * CA No. A20330.


* Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Clifford B. Olsen, Judge. 55 Or App 113, 637 P2d 217 (1981)



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of a court of appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a trial court's order that upheld a search of defendant's purse, including the opening of a coin compartment of a wallet within the purse, conducted without a warrant, after defendant was arrested for possession of a controlled substance. Defendant challenged the search as violating state and federal constitutional prohibitions against unreasonable searches or seizures.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was arrested for possession of a controlled substance, a police officer took defendant's purse and, on the way to the police station, the officer opened the purse, found a wallet within, opened the coin compartment of the wallet, and found amphetamines. The trial court upheld the search as an inventory search. The lower court upheld the search as incident to defendant's arrest. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the search was incident to a lawful arrest and was therefore an exception to the warrant requirement of the Fourth Amendment and Or. Const. art. 1, § 9. The court held that Oregon no longer followed the federal law regarding the constitutional minimum standard for the protection of privacy in cases of searches incident to arrests and formulated an independent rule consistent with past Oregon decisions, which held that a warrantless search had to be justified by the circumstances surrounding the arrest. Thus, the court held that the search of defendant's purse was justified because it was reasonable to believe under the circumstances that defendant had contraband in her purse and the search was close in time and space to defendant's arrest.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that affirmed the trial court's order, which upheld a search of defendant's purse, including the opening of a coin compartment of a wallet within the purse, conducted without a warrant, after defendant was arrested for possession of a controlled substance.

State v. Buttrey


SC No. 28155


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 575; 651 P.2d 1075; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1027


February 10, 1982, Argued and submitted; September 7, 1982, Reargued  
October 5, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * Nos. C80-10-33782, CC78-424, CA No. A20616.


*  Appeal from Circuit Courts of Multnomah and Clatsop Counties. James R. Ellis and Donald L. Kalberer, Judges. 54 Or App 40, 642 P2d 704 (1981).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the trial court (Oregon), which convicted her of operating a motor vehicle without a valid license in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560. The trial court suspended imposition of defendant's sentence and placed defendant on five years' probation and required her to serve one year in the county jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with operating a motor vehicle without a valid driver's license in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560. Following a trial, defendant moved for acquittal. The trial court denied the motion and convicted defendant of the crime charged. Upon review, the court affirmed. The court held that § 487.560 and the legislative history indicated that the legislature intended to create a strict liability crime and as such the prosecution was not required to prove a culpable mental state. The court found, however, the legislature allowed a defendant to establish a limited defense that she did receive notice of the suspension of her license as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.570, or that she did not receive notice in accordance with Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560(3)(c). The court determined the limited defense was unavailable if the state could establish that the defendant had actual knowledge of the suspension. The court concluded a provision under Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560(2) that shifted the burden to defendant to establish she did not receive notice of the suspension of her license was not unconstitutional where knowledge of the suspension was not an element of the crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that convicted defendant of operating a motor vehicle without a valid license.

 

277 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Wedge


SC No. 28477


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 598; 652 P.2d 773; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 1031


July 14, 1982, Argued and submitted  
October 5, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. A21873.


*  Appeal from judgment of the Circuit Court for Washington County, Honorable Donald C. Ashmanskas, Judge. (TC 20-710). 55 Or App 1029, 643 P2d 423 (1982).



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Court of Appeals is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following defendant's conviction for robbery in a jury trial, the trial court imposed a minimum five-year sentence for the use of a firearm pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610(4), even though the jury did not necessarily find that defendant had personally used or threatened to use a firearm. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Three men robbed the victims. Two carried guns and the other carried a knife. The indictments were worded in such a way that defendant could have been convicted of all of the offenses whether he was carrying one of the guns or the knife. Defendant was convicted on all counts. Defendant's convictions were merged and he was sentenced to 20 years' imprisonment for robbery in the first degree. At the sentence proceeding, the circuit court found beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant had used or threatened to use a firearm and imposed a five-year minimum term of imprisonment pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610(4). Defendant appealed only that five-year minimum term. The court held that the term was unconstitutional, reversed, and remanded for resentencing. The court stated that the finding by the circuit court, rather than by a jury, of defendant's personal use of a firearm violated his constitutional right to a trial by jury guaranteed by Or. Const. art. I, § 11 because the firearm finding went to the criminal act for which defendant was punished, and thus was closer to an element of the crime than to a characterization of defendant.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

State v. Robertson


SC Nos. 28280, 28281


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 402; 649 P.2d 569; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 975

August 3, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * TC Nos. 10-80-07971, 10-80-07969, CA Nos. 19337, 19338.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County, James R. Hargreaves, Judge. 54 Or App 630, 635 P2d 1057 (1981) (Robertson); 54 Or App 681, 635 P2d 681 (1981) (Young)



DISPOSITION: Decision of Court of Appeals is reversed. Remand to circuit court for entry of judgment.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were indicted under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.275(1)(e), which made it a crime to compel or induce another person to engage in conduct from which he has the legal right to abstain by causing him to fear the disclosure of discreditable assertions about some person. Defendants appealed the decision of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which reversed the trial court's decision to sustain defendant's demurrer.


OVERVIEW: The state argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.275(1)(e), the specific provision under which the present charge was brought, could be saved if it was interpreted to forbid only threats to make defamatory disclosures if the threatener's demand was not met. However, the court held that it could not escape the conclusion that the statute as written reached areas of constitutionally privileged expression and thus was invalid unless its coverage was narrowed to exclude these areas. The court further held that in the case of this statute the needed narrowing could not be accomplished by judicial interpretation. Therefore, the court concluded that the circuit court did not err in sustaining defendants' demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for entry of a judgment sustaining defendants' demurrer.

Pacific First Federal Sav. & Loan Asso. v. Department of Revenue


SC No. 27631


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 138; 645 P.2d 27; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 885


October 6, 1981, Argued and submitted  
May 19, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Oregon Tax Court. * Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. TC No. 1359.


*  8 OTR 466 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Oregon Department of Revenue, sought review of an order of the Oregon Tax Court, which upheld Pub. L. No. 93-100, 87 Stat. 347 (1973), as constitutional in favor of respondent taxpayer, a savings and loan association. The Department had assessed a deficiency after the taxpayer filed a corporate excise tax return stating that it earned no taxable income in Oregon pursuant to the statute.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer had filed an Oregon corporate excise tax return with the defendant Department stating that it earned no taxable income in Oregon, and the Department assessed a deficiency. The Department sought to have the statute exempting the "doing business" state tax held the unconstitutional, contending that the statute was beyond the powers granted to Congress. The taxpayer contended that Congress was empowered to act either under its admitted power to exempt "federal instrumentalities" from state taxation or under its power to regulate Commerce among the several states under U.S. Const. art. I, § 8. The court upheld the judgment granted to the taxpayer. Congress was empowered to act by the Commerce Clause. There was a "rational basis" for Congress to believe that states' "doing business" taxes levied on concerns such as the taxpayer were on activities in interstate commerce or activities affecting interstate commerce. The exemption only applied to the taxing of depositories that had their principal office in another state. It was rational to conclude that the spatial arrangement probably would have involved or affected transactions and the flow of property across state lines.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granted to the taxpayer.

State v. Roberti


SC No. 27840


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


293 Ore. 59; 644 P.2d 1104; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 863


September 4, 1981, Argued and submitted; April 6, 1982, Reargued  
May 4, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Allowed. Decision Withdrawn. Court of Appeals Affirmed. Remanded to District Court June 22, 1982 (293 Or 236, 646 P2d 1341). U.S. Certiorari Allowed, Remanded to Oregon Supreme Court July 5, 1984 (82 L Ed2d 873).


PRIOR HISTORY: On Review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. T79-12-0348, CA No. 18838.


** Appeal from District Court, Clackamas County. Joseph Thalhofer, Judge pro tempore. 51 Or App 783, 627 P2d 28 (1981).



DISPOSITION: The Court of Appeals is reversed; the district court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicants. The appellate court held that defendant's conviction was based on a statement defendant made after the officer had formed, but not communicated, his intention to arrest defendant. The court held that the statement was the product of custodial interrogation and should have been suppressed.


OVERVIEW: When defendant was stopped by the arresting officer, he was given field sobriety tests. Afterward, the officer determined that he was going to arrest defendant for driving while under the influence of intoxicants. The officer had not yet communicated that intention to defendant, when he asked defendant to rate himself on a scale of intoxication of 0 to 10, 0 being not intoxicated and 10 being extremely intoxicated. Defendant responded that he was a "low five" and that he should not have been driving and should have let another passenger drive. On appeal, defendant challenged the admission of his statements on grounds that it violated his 5th and 14th Amendment rights because he had not first received Miranda warnings. The court agreed with the state's assertion that the Miranda definition of custody referred to restrictions of greater magnitude and apparent duration than exist in an ordinary investigative traffic stop such as defendant's. Therefore, the court concluded that the statements in issue were properly admitted because they were the product of noncustodial questioning and because there was no evidence of the statements' involuntariness in the totality of the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for driving under the influence of intoxicants.

State v. Blake

*  Appeal from Clackamas County. Dale Jacobs, Judge. 53 Or App 906, 633 P2d 831 (1981).



DISPOSITION: Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which held that the state's rape shield law, Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.475, did not violate Or. Const. art. I, §§ 10 or 11. The statute provided that a hearing to determine what evidence of a victim's prior sexual conduct would be admissible at trial would be held out of the presence of the jury and the public.


OVERVIEW: Over defendant's objection, the trial court excluded everyone from a hearing under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.475 except court officers; witnesses, including the alleged victim; the attorneys; and defendant. Defendant was subsequently convicted. On appeal, the court held that (1) it was unnecessary to consider statutory changes that had been made since defendant's trial, and that even though the challenged statutory scheme had been modified the court likely would have granted review if it appeared likely that defendant had been prejudiced by the application of an arguably unconstitutional statute; (2) defendant did not contend that any additional or different evidence would have been introduced if the hearing had been public or that the trial court at trial erred in excluding any evidence concerning the victim's previous sexual conduct.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the appellate proceeding.
