Johnson v. Department of Revenue


SC No. 27708


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


292 Ore. 373; 639 P.2d 128; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 708


November 2, 1981, Argued and submitted  
January 19, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Oregon Tax Court, Judge Carlisle B. Roberts. Tax Court No. 1334.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Oregon Tax Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, taxpayer and a lessor of property owned by the State of Oregon, sought review of a judgment by the Oregon Tax Court, which upheld respondent county tax assessor's assessment that certain submerged and submersible land was subject to ad valorem property taxation. The taxpayer was the owner in fee simple of the dry land.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer contended that the assessment of the leased property was contrary to the legislative intent in enacting Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.110 providing for taxation of state owned lands leased to individuals, and on the ground that it violated the Or. Const. art. VII, § 5. The taxpayer contested that the application of Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.1110 on two grounds. First, the taxpayer argued that principles of statutory construction must be applied to Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.110 to effectuate the policy underlying the statutory scheme, namely, maximization of the common school fund. Second, the taxpayer challenged the validity of Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.110 under the Oregon Const. art. VIII, § 5, requiring that common school funds be managed with the object of obtaining the greatest benefit for the people of Oregon. In affirming, the court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.110 did not violate the Oregon Constitution. The court concluded that when state owned property was used for public purpose it was exempt from taxation, but where public property was used for private purposes, the policy underlying the exemption from taxation no longer applied.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

283 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Emerald People's Utility Dist. v. Joseph


SC No. 28313


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


292 Ore. 357; 640 P.2d 1011; 1982 Ore. LEXIS 709


January 11, 1982, Argued and submitted  
January 15, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ dismissed. Costs and attorney fees to neither party.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was an original proceeding in mandamus that was instituted by plaintiff public utility that sought to compel defendant appellate judge to vacate an appellate court order that denied the public utility's motion to expedite the briefing schedule in two appeals.


OVERVIEW: The public utility had commenced a special proceeding that challenged the validity of a revenue bond election, which resulted in judgments in the public utility's favor. Or. Rev. Stat. § 261.615 provided that an appeal from such a special proceeding had to be heard within three months. The appellate court had denied the public utility's motion to expedite the briefs on the appeal. The public utility then filed a writ in mandamus that sought to compel the appellate court to expedite the briefs on the appeal. On appeal, the court found the legislative command to the appellate court for expediting an appeal for special proceedings did not unduly burden or unduly interfere with the judiciary in the exercise of its judicial functions. The court held that the appellate court was in error in disregarding the legislative command in § 261.615 when considering the public utility's motion to expedite. Nonetheless, the court declined to issue the writ in mandamus because to require such an action at this late stage could unduly interfere with the appellate court's schedule.


OUTCOME: The court issued a judgment that dismissed the public utility's writ in mandamus and declined to compel the appellate court judge to vacate an appellate court order that denied the public utility's motion to expedite the briefing schedule in two appeals.

 

284 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Newton


SC No. 27149


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 788; 636 P.2d 393; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 1125


October 7, 1980, Argued and submitted; July 1, 1981, Reargued  
October 27, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. M79-783, CA No. 16311.


**  Appeal from District Court, Douglas County. Ronald Poole, Judge. 46 Or App 491, 611 P2d 657 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed the trial court's grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence of a Breathalyzer test of defendant's blood alcohol content (BAC) in the prosecution for driving under the influence of intoxicants in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540.


OVERVIEW: The state challenged the appeals court's affirmance of the grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence of a Breathalyzer test. The trial court suppressed the evidence on the basis that defendant was entitled to a telephone call to consult a lawyer before taking the test. The court reversed the appeals court judgment. The court held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540, the drunk driving statute, was an implied consent statute that authorized police to overcome refusal to take the Breathalyzer test by threat of adverse consequences. The court held that § 487.540 was not violated and that there was no violation of defendant's rights under U.S. Const. amends. IV, VI. The court held that defendant's right to counsel did not attach before formal charges were filed. The court also held that the test was a warrantless search, but was reasonable because the fact that the alcohol dissipates constituted exigent circumstances. The court held that defendant's rights under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment were violated - in that his general privacy rights to communicate with others, including his attorney, were restricted, but that he was not prejudiced by the error.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appeals court judgment that affirmed the grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence of a Breathalyzer test and held that the evidence could be used at trial. The case was remanded.

 

285 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Burlington Northern, Inc. v. Department of Revenue


SC No. 26684


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 729; 635 P.2d 347; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 1109


October 7, 1980, Argued and submitted  
October 20, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Oregon Tax Court. * Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. TC Nos. 1098, 1181.


*  8 OTR 19 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant railroad sought review of the decision of the Oregon Tax Court, which granted judgment for defendant Department of Revenue (Department), in the railroad's action protesting the Department's annual assessment of the railroad's transportation property.


OVERVIEW: The Department assessed taxes on the railroad's transportation property under Or. Rev. Stat. § 308.15. The railroad contested the assessment. The trial court generally agreed with the Department's method of computation of the income to be considered, but withheld the entry of its decree pending a recomputation by the Department. The court modified the trial court's decision. The Department's income approach valuation using an annuity technique was not appropriate to the valuation of the railroad's ongoing operations. The income approach was a method of estimating the present worth of benefits to be derived from property in the future. It involved a determination of the present and prospective income from the property, reduced to an indication of value by a mathematical process known as capitalization. Direct capitalization, not based on the life expectancy of the railroad's assets, was appropriate, because the railroad was required to provide reasonably adequate service, equipment, and facilities under § 760.015, and federal law prohibited them from discontinuing service without a governmental determination that such discontinuance was in the public interest.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's decision in the railroad's action protesting the Department's annual assessment of the railroad's transportation property.

 

286 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


McCall v. Legislative Assembly


Nos. 28016, 28042, 28045, 28061, 28064, 27946, 28054


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 663; 634 P.2d 223; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 1090


September 14, 1981, Argued and submitted  
September 23, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] In the Matter of the Review of 1981 Or Laws, ch 261, Relating to the Apportionment of Senators and Representatives.



DISPOSITION: 1981 Or Laws, ch 261, Relating to the Apportionment of Senators and Representatives does not comply with subsection (1) of Or Const, Art IV, section 6, and is therefore null and void.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner voters sought review in the court of 1981 Or. Laws 261 relating to the apportionment of senators and representatives, arguing that it violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 188.010 and the 14th Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The people of the state adopted a constitutional amendment to Or. Const. art. IV, § 6 that would assure them of an apportionment of the legislative assembly after each federal census. The amendment provided for review of challenges to a reapportionment measure enacted by the assembly, for preparation of a reapportionment by the Secretary of State if the assembly enacted no reapportionment measure or enacted a faulty one, and for further review by the court of any reapportionment thus prepared by the secretary. The voters challenged 1981 Or. Laws 261 on a variety of grounds, including the formation of a new district by adding some adjoining census tracts to an existing district to meet the new population ratio. The voters argued that the law divided a city and a county into separate legislative districts and that certain residents would not be able to vote in some elections. The court held that the reapportionment complied with the 14th Amendment standards of equal representation, but did not comply with Or. Const. art. IV, § 6(1), and was therefore null and void. The court ordered the secretary to prepare a draft reapportionment and return the draft to the court.


OUTCOME: The court held that the reapportionment complied with the 14th Amendment but did not comply with the state constitution, in an action brought by the voters to challenge a reapportionment measure enacted by the legislative assembly.

 

287 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Blocker


SC No. 27255


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 255; 630 P.2d 824; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 915


December 2, 1980, Argued and submitted  
June 30, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. 15586.


*  Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County (DA 166838-7903). C. X. Bollenback, Judge. 47 Or App 376, 614 P2d 1216 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed defendant's conviction for possessing a "billy" in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) on the grounds that § 166.510(1) was unconstitutional under Or. Const. art. I, § 27, which afforded defendant the right to bear arms.


OVERVIEW: At trial, the evidence showed that defendant was stopped by a police officer for driving at an excessive speed whereupon the officer observed a wooden "billy" on the floor of the car. Defendant told the officer that he made the club in a high school shop class and used it to hit people who hassled him. At trial, he argued that Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) was unconstitutional because it was overbroad, but he did not assert his right to bear arms. The appeals court reversed defendant's conviction, ruling that § 166.510(1) contravened his constitutional right to bear arms. On appeal, the court ruled that under Or. Const. art. I, § 27, defendant had the right to bear arms, and that right necessarily included the right to possess a "billy." Thus, the court held, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1) was an impermissible prohibition on defendant's constitutionally protected right to bear arms. The court also ruled that because defendant's argument that § 166.510(1) was overbroad was, in effect, an assertion that the statute swept so broadly as to infringe his constitutional right to possess arms, the appeals court properly considered Or. Const. art. I, § 27 and reversed defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appeals court's order, which reversed defendant's conviction.

State v. Shumway


SC No. 26922


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 153; 630 P.2d 796; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 913


September 10, 1980, Argued and submitted; April 7, 1981, Reargued  
June 23, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * No. 79-2-17, CA No. 14464.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Winston L. Bradshaw, Judge. 44 Or App 657, 607 P2d 191 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Sentence modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Oregon Court of Appeals, which affirmed both his conviction for intentional murder under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115 and his sentence for life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly shot the unarmed victim as he approached defendant in a threatening manner. After the victim fell to the ground, defendant fired a second shot, which was the homicidal act. Defendant was convicted of intentional murder under § 163.115 and sentenced to life imprisonment. An initiative amended § 163.115 and required defendant to serve 25 years of his life sentence before becoming eligible for parole. The court of appeals affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that any error in the trial court's self-defense instruction was harmless because the issue of self-defense was not presented by the evidence. However, § 163.115 and the aggravated murder statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.105, were not repugnant, thus, the initiative did not impliedly repeal § 163.105. Thus, the statutory scheme violated Or. Const. art. I, § 16 because a lesser minimum sentence was imposed before being eligible for parole for aggravated intentional homicide than for unaggravated intentional homicide, and the Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5) provision requiring defendant to serve not less than 25 years before becoming eligible for parole was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals that affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for intentional murder.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed the rejection of defendant's motion to dismiss an indictment against him for theft in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted pursuant to a district attorney's information. The State offered immunity to several of defendant's co-offenders. The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment against him.

290 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Curran


SC Nos. 27038, 27046


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


291 Ore. 119; 628 P.2d 1198; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 845


October 7, 1980, Argued and submitted  
June 2, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. ** 


**  Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County (No. 78-11-122) Dale Jacobs, Judge, 45 Or App 859, 609 P2d 427.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a convicted felon, and respondent State petitioned for review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a trial court's forfeiture order on the grounds that a trial by jury was required and remanded the matter for a jury trial. The court allowed the petitions to consider the basis for appellate jurisdiction and whether a trial by jury was required in the post-conviction forfeiture proceeding.


OVERVIEW: After the felon was convicted by a jury for unlawful possession of a controlled substance, cocaine, the district attorney filed a motion under Or. Rev. Stat. § 167.247(2) for an order forfeiting a vehicle that allegedly belonged to the felon. The trial court held that a jury trial was not required and, after a hearing, ordered the vehicle forfeited. The court asked the parties to address the basis for appellate jurisdiction in the case. Affirming the court of appeals' judgment, the court found that the forfeiture proceeding was not a criminal action and that the appeal was not from a judgment on a conviction; rather, it was an appeal from a final order affecting a substantial right that was made in a proceeding after judgment or decree and was reviewable under Or. Rev. Stat. § 19.010. The court agreed with the court of appeals that the felon was entitled to a jury trial because Or. Const. art I, § 17 required a jury trial in a forfeiture proceeding where issues of fact germane to the forfeiture were not resolved in the underlying criminal action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals, which reversed the trial court's forfeiture order and remanded the cause for a jury trial.

Sterling v. Cupp


SC Nos. 26907, 26915


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, prison officials and guards, sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's order that enjoined petitioner officials from assigning female correctional officers to any position in which the job description or actual duties include frisks or pat-downs of male prisoners, except in emergency situations.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, male prison inmates, sued to enjoin petitioner prison officials from assigning female guards to duties which involve frisking male prisoners or observation of prisoners in showers or toilets. The trial court enjoined petitioner officials assigning female correctional officers to any position in which the job description or actual duties include frisks or pat-downs of male prisoners, except in emergency situations. 292 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Duggan


SC No. 27221


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


290 Ore. 369; 622 P.2d 316; 1981 Ore. LEXIS 675


December 3, 1980, Argued and submitted  
January 20, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * No. T66761, CA No. 16704.


*  Appeal from Judgment of the District Court for Linn County, William O. Lewis, Judge. 47 Or App 219, 613 P2d 1110 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirming his conviction for driving a motor vehicle while his operator's license was suspended in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560.


OVERVIEW: At the time of his arrest, defendant was steering a vehicle being towed by another vehicle on a public highway. He appealed his conviction for operating a motor vehicle while under suspension, contending that his conduct did not constitute the "driving" of a "motor vehicle" for the purposes of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560. The appellate court affirmed. Defendant petitioned for review, contending that the appellate court erred in rejecting, without discussion, defendant's contention that the vehicle being towed was not a "motor vehicle" for the purposes of § 487.560. The court noted that Or. Rev. Stat. § 483.014 defined the term "motor vehicle" as any vehicle "which is self-propelled" and concluded that this statute was ambiguous when applied to a situation involving a vehicle under tow. Because the statute in question was penal in nature, the court observed that defendant was entitled to the benefit of the more lenient construction of it. The court concluded that the definition of "motor vehicle," as applied to persons who steered or braked vehicles under tow, meant a vehicle which was in fact "self-propelled." Thus, defendant was not driving a "motor vehicle."


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court and defendant's conviction were reversed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the denial of his suppression motion by the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged in the alternative with intentional murder and felony murder and convicted of the former. He was also charged with and convicted of burglary. However, the court reversed defendant's death sentence because imposition of a greater penalty denied defendant his right to trial by jury.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress.

Adams v. Psychiatric Sec. Review Bd.


SC No. 27058


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


290 Ore. 273; 621 P.2d 572; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1292


September 8, 1980, Argued and submitted  
December 30, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * No. 79-189, CA No. 15065.


*  Judicial Review from Psychiatric Security Review Board. 45 Or App 997, 609 P2d 998 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent psychiatric security review board sought review of an order by the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which vacated a decision by the board committing petitioner patient to a state hospital for custody, care, and treatment.


OVERVIEW: The patient was found not guilty of murder by reason of mental disease or defect. The trial court found that the patient was substantially dangerous to her herself and others and placed her under the jurisdiction of the board, ordering her committed to a state hospital pending a hearing before the board. At its initial hearing, the board concluded that the patient could not have been adequately controlled if she were conditionally released and ordered her to be committed to the state hospital. The appellate court vacated the board's order. On review, the court held that the appellate court erred by requiring the board to find at its initial hearing substantial evidence of the patient's mental disease or defect and dangerousness. The court stated that the board's duty at the initial hearing was only to commit or conditionally release the patient. The court noted, as evidence that the board was not required to make an independent finding of dangerousness, that the trial court was required to do so no more than 20 days before the initial hearing, and that either of the board's determinations at the initial hearing were predicated on a previous finding of dangerousness.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court.

State v. Stroup


SC No. 27010


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


290 Ore. 185; 620 P.2d 1359; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1236


October 7, 1980, Argued and submitted  
December 9, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 13, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Review from the Court of Appeals. ** No. 78-32999, CA No. 16139.


**  Appeal from a judgment of the District Court for Lane County, Gregory G. Foote, Judge. 45 Or App 543, 608 P2d 625 (1980).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirming defendant's conviction for driving a motor vehicle while his operator's license had been suspended in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the State failed to prove that the Motor Vehicle Division had mailed to him notice that his license had been suspended. On further appeal, the court held that no agency rules required notice of the entry of an order of suspension before the suspension was valid. Defendant's license was suspended "for failure to appear" in court. Therefore, notice that defendant's license might have been suspended for "failure to appear" would have been provided by the mailing of a notice pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 484.210. Or. Admin. R. 137-03-000 did not require the mailing of notice after the entry of an order of suspension. The court also found that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 482.570, 487.560 did not require that notice of the entry of an order of suspension for failure to appear be mailed before the suspension of the license was valid. There was no language in Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.560 stating that for an order suspending a driver's license to have been valid, notice of the suspension must have been subsequently mailed pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.570. The State only had to prove the suspension and the driving while suspended, and did not have to prove the mailing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the trial court convicting defendant of driving a motor vehicle while his operator's license had been suspended.

 

296 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Application of Haynes


SC No. 27083


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


290 Ore. 75; 619 P.2d 632; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1153


June 25, 1980, Argued and submitted  
November 4, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Writ of Habeas Corpus. TC No. 78-1763.



DISPOSITION: Plaintiff remanded to custody of defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate, incarcerated while awaiting trial on a murder charge, filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus, seeking to be released from imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was incarcerated for two and one-half years while awaiting trial on a charge of murder. The inmate filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus, arguing that the denial of her application for security release contravened the governing law and that she was entitled to have the charge against her dismissed because she had been denied a speedy trial. Upon consideration, the court denied the inmate's application. The court found that the inmate was not entitled to a security release under Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.240(2), noting that the trial court had reasonably determined that there was a strong likelihood that she would have been convicted at trial. Although noting that the inmate had been incarcerated for an extraordinary length of time, the court concluded that the inmate had not been denied a speedy trial. The court found that the inmate herself was responsible for a significant portion of the delay in bringing the matter to trial, that there was no question of bad faith on the part of the prosecution, and that the inmate had failed to prove that the delay had adversely affected her ability to present a viable defense.


OUTCOME: The court denied plaintiff's application for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

297 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Stevenson v. State Dep't of Transp.


SC Nos. 26610, 26623


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


290 Ore. 3; 619 P.2d 247; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1143


March 4, 1980, Argued and submitted  
November 4, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. 76-956-L, CA No. 10590.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Josephine County. Samuel M. Bowe, Judge. 42 Or App 747, 601 P2d 854 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Reversed with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner personal representative brought an action against respondent state for wrongful death and cross-petitioner driver brought a personal injury action against the state following a collision at an intersection that was controlled by a traffic light. The trial court rendered judgment for the personal representative and the driver. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed. The personal representative and driver appealed.


OVERVIEW: The personal representative and the driver asserted that the state was liable either because signal lights, at the time of the accident, were malfunctioning or because the arrangement and design of the signals were deceptive to oncoming motorists. The jury returned a verdict for the personal representative and the driver. The appellate court reversed and held that there was no evidence from which the jury could have found that the lights had malfunctioned. On appeal, the court held that: (1) there was evidence from which the jury could have inferred that the lights had malfunctioned and that the state was negligent by not correcting the malfunction; (2) the state and its employees were shielded from liability by Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.265(3) for a claim that was based on a discretionary function of government, interpreted as involving policy type decisions; (3) Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.285 required the state to defend and indemnify its employees for tort claims based on their public duties in the absence of aggravated malfeasance; and (4) the burden was on the state to establish its immunity and it had not done so where it conceded that its engineering judgment was negligently exercised.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment from the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's judgment in favor of the personal representative and the driver in their wrongful death and negligent actions against the state. The court remanded the matter with instructions to reinstate the trial court's judgment.

Klamath Falls v. Winters


SC Nos. 26587, 26611


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 757; 619 P.2d 217; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1134


March 3, 1980, Argued and submitted  
October 21, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied November 20, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On Review from the Court of Appeals. * No. 76-259 C, CA No. 12533.


*  Appeal from Judgment of the Circuit Court for Klamath County, Theodore Abram, Judge. 42 Or App 223, 600 P2d 478 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both parties, defendants and petitioner city, sought review of a judgment of the court of appeals (Oregon), which affirmed defendants' convictions for disorderly conduct by the circuit court but held that a municipal ordinance that precluded appeal to the court of appeals was unconstitutional as a denial of equal protection of the laws.


OVERVIEW: The city contended that Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 221.350, 221.360 restricted the right of persons convicted in municipal court to a de novo appeal in circuit court and only allowed further appeal to the court of appeals in cases involving the constitutionality of a charter provision or ordinance under which a conviction was obtained. Defendants contended that the restricted right of appeal in cases originating in municipal court denied them equal protection of the laws, and that they were also denied equal protection of the laws because the police had unlimited discretion to prosecute them in municipal court, rather than in district court. The court found that the court of appeals erred in its holding that Or. Rev. Stat. § 221.360, to the extent it provided for a reduced scope of appellate court review, violated the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and Or. Const. art. I, § 20. The court of appeals had no jurisdiction to consider those questions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of appeals which ruled that a municipal ordinance that precluded appeal to the court of appeals was unconstitutional and the convictions of defendants for disorderly conduct were affirmed.

 

299 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tourtillott


SC No. 26660


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 845; 618 P.2d 423; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1136


January 8, 1980, Argued and submitted  
October 21, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied November 25, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from Court of Appeals. * No. 10158, CA No. 10998.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court for Coos County. John C. Warden, Judge. 43 Or App 5, 602 P2d 659 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed her conviction for driving with a revoked license after she was detained at a game checkpoint stop.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was detained at a game checkpoint stop. When the officer asked for identification, defendant admitted that she was driving with a revoked license. The court affirmed defendant's conviction for driving with a revoked license. The court determined that game checkpoint stops where there was neither probable cause to believe nor reasonable suspicion that a game law, or any other law, had been violated. The court determined that Or. Rev. Stat. § 496.610 gave the state police the power to enforce wildlife laws. The police set up a roadblock to check hunters' compliance with the laws, check hunting licenses, and to gather statistics. Under Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.615 an officer could not stop a person without reasonable suspicion. The court held that the legislature did not intend to limit all stops for law enforcement purposes to those permitted under § 131.615 and that checkpoint stops did not fall under that provision. The court held that the checkpoint did not violate the constitution because the officers did not have unconstrained discretion, and the procedure was effective in meeting its ultimate goal of enforcing hunting laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision affirming defendant's conviction for driving with a revoked license after she was detained at a game checkpoint stop.

State v. Reynolds

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence for aggravated felony murder in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095(2)(d).


OVERVIEW: Defendant and a friend shot a man while robbing a tavern. Defendant was convicted of aggravated felony murder by the trial court. The appellate court affirmed the judgment and defendant appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court. The court found that the evidence indicated that defendant had personally committed the homicide. The prosecutor sought, and the grand jury returned, an indictment-charging defendant with aggravated felony murder. The court found no merit to defendant's contention that prosecutorial discretion in determining whether to charge defendant with aggravated felony murder or felony murder was a patent violation of equal protection. The court ruled that a prosecutor's decision as to which distinct offense to charge a defendant with did not alone constitute a grant of unequal privileges or immunities or a denial of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, which affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of aggravated felony murder.

 

301 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Kessler


SC No. 26705


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 359; 614 P.2d 94; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1031


March 4, 1980, Argued and submitted  
July 15, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * TC DA No. 160004-7811, CA No. 14296.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County Philip T. Abraham, Judge 43 Or App 303, 602 P2d 1096 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgments of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him for disorderly conduct and possession of a slugging weapon in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.510(1). Defendant claimed that the legislative prohibition of the possession of a billy club violated Or. Const. art. I., § 27.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in a verbal argument with his apartment manager that escalated into name calling, colorful words, and object throwing. After defendant kicked the elevator door, police arrested defendant. While retrieving defendant's coat from his apartment, police discovered two billy clubs in the apartment. Defendant was charged with and convicted of disorderly conduct and possession of a slugging weapon. The lower appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. Although defendant did not raise at trial the contention that his acts did not amount to the crime of disorderly conduct, the court affirmed the disorderly conduct conviction upon determining that defendant's case did not present the exceptional circumstance or manifest error which justified the court's consideration of his claim. The court reversed defendant's conviction for possession of a billy club. The court held that Or. Const. art. I, § 27 included a right to possess certain arms for defense of person and property and that defendant's possession of a billy club, an effective, hand-carried weapon or arm commonly used for personal defense, was constitutionally protected.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for disorderly conduct and reversed his conviction for possession of a slugging weapon.

 

302 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Oregonian Pub. Co. v. Deiz


SC No. 26832


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 277; 613 P.2d 23; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 1077; 6 Media L. Rep. 1369


March 26, 1980, Argued and submitted  
June 18, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ to issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, newspaper publisher and reporter, filed a petition for a writ of mandamus to direct defendant judge to allow them to attend all hearings in a juvenile court proceedings in which a juvenile was in custody in connection with the drowning of a child.


OVERVIEW: The press was barred from the courtroom during the juvenile's hearings. The newspaper publisher filed a motion to be permitted to attend the hearings. The judge denied the motion and barred the reporter from the hearings. The newspaper publisher and reporter petitioned for a writ of mandamus. They contended that the application of Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.498(1), which granted broad authority to the judge to control access to the courtroom, was invalid because it was contrary to Or. Const. art I, § 10. The court issued the writ, holding that the order of the judge barring the newspaper publisher and reporter from the hearings was invalid under art. I, § 10. Article I, § 10 stated that no court could be secret, but justice would be administered openly and without delay. It did not provide that the accused or anyone else has the right to a public trial. It stated that all proceedings before the courts were required to be open to the public.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandamus directing the judge to permit the newspaper publisher and reporter to attend subsequent proceedings in the underlying juvenile proceeding.

 

303 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Spencer


SC No. 26493


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 225; 611 P.2d 1147; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 926


February 5, 1980, Argued and submitted  
June 3, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. B47-427, CA No. 13453.


*  Appeal from District Court, Lane County. Gregory G. Foote, Judge. 41 Or App 393, 599 P2d 464 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged by the State with violation of the disorderly conduct statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.025(1)(c), for using abusive and obscene language in a public place. Defendant filed a demurrer in which he attacked the constitutionality of § 166.025(1)(c) on its face. The trial court sustained the demurrer. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) reversed and remanded for trial. The court allowed review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed the language that defined the offense was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad in that it could deter citizens from exercising their right of free expression guaranteed by Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The court reversed the court of appeals. Section 166.025(1)(c) made the expression or the speaking of certain kinds of language criminal if done intentionally or recklessly to cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm. The statute did not require that the words spoken actually cause public inconvenience, annoyance, or alarm, but made the speaking of the words themselves criminal if spoken with the requisite intent even if no harm was caused or threatened. The statute did restrain freedom of expression of opinion and restricted the right to speak. When the terms of a statute as written prohibited or restrained expression that came within the protection of Or. Const. art. I, § 8, the statute was a law that was forbidden. Freedom of speech did not exclude expressions that were offensive or obscene in the sense of vulgar and the phrase "obscene language" in the statute was not limited to words that appealed to a prurient interest in sex, which would not be protected.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed and the order sustaining defendant's demurrer was reinstated.

 

304 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Automotive Emporium, Inc. v. Murchison


SC Nos. 26477, 26478


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


289 Ore. 265; 611 P.2d 1169; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 930


February 6, 1980, Argued and submitted  
June 3, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied (289 Or 673, 616 P2d 496) September 23, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Alternative writ of mandamus dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, indicted criminal relators, petitioned for a writ of mandamus to mandate that defendants, circuit judge and district attorney, conduct a preliminary hearing or dismiss the charges. Defendants asserted that mandamus was not a proper remedy because relators might have appealed if they were convicted.


OVERVIEW: Relators contended that direct appeal was not an adequate remedy because they would have been prejudiced in two respects if forced to trial without a preliminary hearing: they would have been denied pretrial discovery of the testimony of a prosecution witness and a pretrial opportunity to determine whether the state had probable cause to require them to answer to the charge. The court concluded that this did not constitute such prejudice as rendered direct appeal a less than adequate remedy and there was an absence of special circumstances, and denied the writ. Relators were not prejudiced by the requirement that they defer review of their claim of denial of discovery until direct appeal, and the prospect of suffering the burden of litigation was not a sufficient injury in itself to justify mandamus. Direct appeal was an adequate remedy unless relator would have suffered a special loss beyond the burden of litigation by being forced to trial. The court held that because direct appeal was a plain, speedy, and adequate remedy for the review of the ruling challenged by relators, mandamus was inappropriate.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the alternative writ of mandamus that relators had petitioned for against defendants to require a preliminary hearing or dismissal the charges against them, and denied relief.

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, individuals and a corporation, sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a ruling that a charter amendment and taxing ordinances, Eugene, Or., City Charter ch. VII, § 48(70) (1973), by respondent city were constitutional and valid.


OVERVIEW: An amendment to a city charter was approved empowering the city council to establish boundaries of a downtown development district for purposes of establishing public parking facilities and imposing taxes upon persons property and businesses to promote economic development. REILLEY v. SECRETARY OF STATE


SC No. 26433


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 573; 607 P.2d 162; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 745

*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Richard D. Barber, Judge. 41 Or App 293, 598 P2d 323 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers and intervenor-petitioner Calckamas County Commissioners (Oregon) sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a summary judgment of the trial court, which dismissed their action against respondent Secretary of State (Oregon) contending that Or. Laws § 665, which permitted the establishment of a metropolitan service district, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers initiated an action against the Secretary contending that Or. Laws § 665 establishing a metropolitan service district was unconstitutional. The trial court allowed the Commissioners to intervene, and dismissed the action in favor of the Secretary granting summary judgment. The taxpayers and the Commissioners sought review. Pursuant to Or. Const. art. XI, § 2, corporations were to be formed under general laws but were not allowed to be created by special laws. The court determined that a special law was one conferring upon certain individuals rights and powers not granted or imposed upon other individuals similarly situated. The court found that the statute had not created the district but concluded that the term was used in the constitution. Therefore, the court held that the statute was not a special law but a general law and was constitutional. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court.

Burke v. Children's Services Div.


SC No. 26253


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 533; 607 P.2d 141; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 742


November 8, 1979, Argued and submitted  
February 20, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied March 18, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals. * No. TC 414-115, CA No. 10501.


*  Appeal from the judgment of the Circuit Court for Multnomah County, Charles S. Crookham, Judge. 39 Or App 819, 593 P2d 1262 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner class of families with dependent children (class members) sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the trial court and denied relief to the class members under a federally funded program that was terminated by defendant children's services division (CSD).


OVERVIEW: The class members sought a declaratory judgment that the CSD improperly terminated a federally funded child care payment program and an order requiring the resumption of payments made to class members. The trial court held that the class members were entitled to a judgment against the CSD in the amounts by which their net available incomes were reduced by the discontinuance of the program. The court of appeals reversed, holding that the trial court exceeded its authority. The CSD terminated the benefit program under a temporary rule, rather than according to procedures prescribed in Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.335. The court held that the CSD failed to repeal the program by rule. The trial court awarded monetary relief when the class members specifically requested only a declaratory judgment that the program had not been properly terminated. The court agreed with the court of appeals that monetary relief could not be granted for an administrative agency's failure to comply with rulemaking provisions of the Administrative Procedures Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 183.400(1) (1975). The court held that declaratory relief was sufficient.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals. Although the class members were entitled to a declaration that the CSD's termination of the child care payment program was ineffective, the trial court did not have authority to grant monetary or injunctive relief to the class members.

 

308 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Scharf


SC No. 25855


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 451; 605 P.2d 690; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 731


April 3, 1979, Argued and submitted; December 3, 1979, Reargued  
January 22, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied February 20, 1980. Separate Opinion on Denial of Petition for Review February 25, 1980 (288 Or 621, 605 P2d 690).


PRIOR HISTORY: On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. T-82694, CA No. 10121.


*  Appeal from District Court, Yamhill County. Donald R. Blensly, Judge. 36 Or App 345, 585 P2d 23 (1978).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon) that affirmed a trial court judgment that convicted her of driving under the influence of intoxicants (DUII) in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.540. Defendant appealed on the ground that the trial court admitted evidence of a chemical test of her breath that was administered after her arrest and after she was denied permission to telephone her attorney for advice.


OVERVIEW: After her arrest for DUII, police officers advised defendant of her choice whether to submit to a chemical test of her blood alcohol level or to accept a four-month license suspension pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 487.805. When defendant asked to telephone her attorney, police officers refused, although no practical obstacles or time exigencies mandated refusal. The court reversed the judgment, determining that the failure to allow the call precluded use of the test results because the state did not obtain the test with defendant's consent. Administration of the breath test was not a civil procedure because a DUII charge was a criminal prosecution. The court held that the defenses available in a license suspension proceeding pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 482.550 for failure to submit to the test and the procedural protections otherwise provided to DUII defendants showed that the legislature intended the choice whether to submit to the test to be an informed one. The legal consequences of the choice were not simple nor within the common knowledge of laymen and the legislature could not have intended to deprive defendants of counsel at the most critical moment in a DUII prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the intermediate appellate court judgment that had affirmed the trial court judgment convicting defendant of DUII. The action was remanded for further proceedings.

 

309 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Megdal v. Oregon State Board of Dental Examiners


SC No. 26120


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 293; 605 P.2d 273; 1980 Ore. LEXIS 727


October 2, 1979, Argued and submitted  
January 2, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * CA No. 9772.


*  Appeal from an Order of the Oregon State Board of Dental Examiners. 38 Or App 469, 590 P2d 745 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner dentist sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which upheld a decision of respondent Oregon State Board of Dental Examiners (Board) revoking his state license on the ground of "unprofessional conduct," pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 679.140(1)(c) and (5)(d).


OVERVIEW: A dentist was licensed both in Oregon and in California, and maintained offices in both states. He sought review of the revocation of his Oregon license on the ground of "unprofessional conduct." The revocation was based on the dentist's act of obtaining malpractice insurance coverage for other dentists employed by him in his California practice by a misrepresentation that they were employed in Oregon. The issue on review was whether the Board could revoke a dentist's license under an unparticularized rubric of "unprofessional conduct" upon an administrative finding that he had practiced a fraud on an insurance company. The court reversed, concluding that the dentist was entitled to relief. His license had been revoked under a statutory standard of "unprofessional conduct," that was broadened beyond its original list of specifications, which the statute meant the Board to particularize by rules. Because there was no such rule made to proscribe the kind of conduct charged against the dentist, there was no legal ground on which to revoke his license. The court noted that the Board had erroneously interpreted the statute, and its error could not have been cured by a remand.


OUTCOME: The order of the court of appeals that upheld the Board's order revoking the dentist's state license was reversed.

State ex rel. Acocella v. Allen


SC Nos. 26296, 26277 Cases Consolidated


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 175; 604 P.2d 391; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1221


September 6, 1979, Argued and submitted  
December 18, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ to issue commanding defendant to appoint counsel; that part of the alternative writ relating to transcripts dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding in mandamus, relator criminal defendants filed petitions for an alternative writ of mandamus, praying that the court order the Lane County circuit judges to appoint counsel other than the Public Defender for their appeals and pay for a complete copy of each of their transcripts.


OVERVIEW: Relators were criminal defendants convicted in the county circuit court of committing various unrelated felonies. Each was represented in the circuit court by appointed counsel, for each, the State Public Defender filed a timely notice of appeal, a motion for appointment of appellate counsel, and for a transcript at county expense, supported by an affidavit attesting to defendant's indigency. The circuit judges refused to permit the Public Defender to decline appointment. The county judges contended that Or. Rev. Stat. § 151.280(7) was unconstitutional, in that it infringed upon the inherent power of the judiciary to compel attorneys as officers of the court to represent indigent defendants. The court issued the writ as to appointing counsel other then the Public Defender, but denied the writ as to the transcripts. The court held that the county was not required to waste public funds by reproducing portions of a transcript that had no relevance to the contentions on appeal. Therefore relators had to outline their arguments so that only the relevant portions of the transcript would be produced.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that a peremptory writ issue to have the county judges appoint counsel other then the public defender and denied a writ requiring the county to provide transcripts.

Fish & Wildlife Dep't v. Land Conservation & Development Com.


SC No. 26018


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 203; 603 P.2d 1371; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1223


July 3, 1979, Argued and submitted  
December 18, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from Court of Appeals. * CA No. 10171.


*  Judicial Review from Oregon Land Conservation and Development Commission and Oregon Land Conservation and Development Department. 37 Or App 607, 588 P2d 80 (1978).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner developer appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a decision of respondent Land Conservation and Development Commission (LCDC) holding that respondent Fish and Wildlife Department had failed to exhaust its administrative remedies before appealing a plat approval to the LCDC. The appellate court had ordered the LCDC to hear the petition.


OVERVIEW: The Department appealed the approval of a plat by the Commission directly to the LCDC rather than to the Board of County Commissioners. The county argued that the Department had failed to exhaust its remedies before seeking review from the LCDC. The LCDC agreed and dismissed the case. The appellate court reversed the dismissal and ordered the LCDC to hear the petition. On further review, the court affirmed, holding that the Department was not required to exhaust remedies because the LCDC had not promulgated such a rule. Further, the legislature had not enacted a law requiring exhaustion. The court did not read the condition into the statutes because exhaustion as a precondition to review by the LCDC was not plainly desirable for accomplishing the legislative purpose. The legislative history of the statute and the function of the LCDC as a monitor of land use decisions also supported the idea that exhaustion could not be read into the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

312 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Dickens v. De Bolt


SC No. 26192


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


288 Ore. 3; 602 P.2d 246; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1206


October 2, 1979, Argued and submitted  
November 6, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. L-6275, CA No. 11338.


*  Appeal from the Judgment of the Circuit Court for Gilliam County, J. R. Campbell, Judge. (39 Or App 575, 592 P2d 1082, (1979)).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff fisherman appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed a judgment for the fisherman in his conversion action against defendant police officer on the ground that the officer was immune from liability pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 496.620.


OVERVIEW: The fisherman caught a sturgeon and tied it to a pier. During the night, the officer confiscated the sturgeon on the ground that it was illegally caught. The officer took the sturgeon to his house and put it in his freezer. There was evidence that the officer ate a portion of the sturgeon. The fisherman filed a conversion action against the officer. The trial court entered a judgment for the fisherman. The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment, holding that the officer was immune from liability pursuant to § 496.620, which granted immunity to officials who enforced wildlife laws. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for a new trial. The court held that the officer was not engaged in the enforcement of wildlife laws when he took the sturgeon to his home and put it in his freezer, so he was not immune from liability pursuant to § 496.620 or § 30.265(3)(c) of the Oregon Tort Claims Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.265(3)(c). However, the court ruled that the trial court improperly instructed the jury on the elements of and defenses to an action for conversion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment for the police officer in the fisherman's conversion action. The court remanded the case to the trial court for a new trial.

State v. Blair


SC No. 26079


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 519; 601 P.2d 766; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1190


September 7, 1979, Argued and submitted  
October 16, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. B46-293, CA No. 12166.


*  Appeal from District Court, Lane County. Maurice K. Merten, Judge. 38 Or App 90, 588 P2d 1132 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed the trial court's judgment that sustained defendant's demurrer to the charge of committing the crime of harassment on the ground that Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(c) was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the crime of committing harassment in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.065(1)(c). The trial court had issued a judgment that sustained defendant's demurrer to the charge on the ground that § 166.065(1)(c) was unconstitutionally vague. On appeal by the state, the appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment. On appeal again by defendant, the court reversed the appellate court's judgment, and reinstated the trial court's judgment. The court examined § 166.065(1)(c) and found the communication proscribed by it need not cause any harm at all, in the form of annoyance, alarm, or otherwise. It was sufficient if the manner of communication was in fact likely to do so, whether defendant knew this or not. The court concluded that the statute, as written, was unconstitutionally vague because it failed to give defendant notice of the conduct that the statute had proscribed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment, and reinstated the trial court's judgment that sustained defendant's demurrer to the charge of committing the crime of harassment on the ground that the Oregon statute was unconstitutionally vague.

 

314 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Reed v. Schwab


SC No. 26199


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 411; 600 P.2d 387; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1180; 24 A.L.R.4th 422


July 2, 1979, Argued and submitted  
September 25, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Writ dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator litigant filed an original mandamus action with the court to compel defendant judicial officials to allow him to present oral argument on his own behalf to the lower court in four cases that were currently pending before the lower court.


OVERVIEW: The litigant was a claimant in four workers' compensation claims that were pending in the lower court. He filed motions for permission to argue his cases orally before the lower court. The lower court citing its internal rule that where a litigant represented himself no oral argument was permitted by any party to the matter, denied his motions and ordered the cases submitted without oral argument. The litigant then brought a mandamus proceeding, claiming that he had a statutory right to argue his own case or, alternately, that such right existed under Or. Const., art I, § 20. The court rejected the litigant's arguments. The court first pointed out that the lower court's rule fell within the authority granted to the lower court by Or. Rev. Stat. § 2.560(6), which authorized the lower court to enact rules that facilitated the processing of appeals before it. The court then found that the Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.230 did not specifically require the lower court to allow oral argument on appeal. The court also found that elimination of oral argument did not violate Or. Const., art I, § 20 that guaranteed that justice be administered openly.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the litigant's request for a writ of mandamus.

Straube v. Emanuel Lutheran Charity Bd.


SC No. 25276


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 375; 600 P.2d 381; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1178


February 6, 1979, Argued and submitted  
September 18, 1979 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied November 20, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Clifford B. Olsen, Judge. No. 402-226.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff radiologist filed an action against defendant hospital in the Circuit Court, Multnomah County, Oregon, alleging wrongful suspension of his staff privileges. The trial court entered judgment in the hospital's favor. The radiologist sought review.


OVERVIEW: The radiologist was excellent in his work but his inability to work with others jeopardized patient care. The radiologist sought an order restoring his staff privileges and enjoining the hospital from future actions unless it complied with due process and hospital bylaws. The radiologist also sought compensatory and punitive damages, attorney fees, and costs. Before trial, the lower court granted the hospital partial summary judgment on the due process issues. In affirming the trial court's judgment, the court concluded that the hospital had complied with its bylaws and that there was a basis in fact for the hospital's suspensions. Further, the court held that Or. Const. art. I, § 10 did not apply to private entities but that the radiologist might have been entitled to common law fair procedure. Without adopting the rule, the court assumed the proposition to decide the instant case and concluded that the hospital adhered to its bylaws which the radiologist agreed to when he accepted staff privileges. The court found that the hospital's decision was made by persons trained in healthcare and that there was no reason for upsetting the decision so long as there was a rationale for it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of judgment in the hospital's favor.

 

316 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Hoffman v. French


SC No. 25927


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 323; 599 P.2d 452; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1010


March 5, 1979, Argued and submitted  
September 11, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. 102347, CA No. 10232.


**  Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Duane R. Ertsgaard, Judge. 36 Or App 739, 585 P2d 730 (1978).



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a defendant in a forcible entry and detainer action in a district court, sought review of an adverse judgment by means of a writ of review in the circuit court. Both the circuit court and the Court of Appeals (Oregon) refused to consider the merits of the petition. Petitioner sought further review of the matter, which was allowed.


OVERVIEW: Both the circuit court and the court of appeals held that the writ of review was not available to review decisions of the district court as an "inferior court" after 1975 Or. Laws 611 made the district court a court of record whose judgments were directly appealable to the court of appeals. The court disagreed and overturned the decision of the court of appeals. At issue was whether circuit court review of district court decisions by means of the writ of review was meant to survive and coexist with the creation of direct appeals from the district court to the court of appeals. The court held that before the enactment of Chapter 611, review by writ of review coexisted with direct "appeals" from the district court to the circuit court. If that was the deliberate legislative policy when an appeal was available to the circuit court, the court held that it was not justified in reading that policy out of Or. Rev. Stat. § 34.040 when the appeal was shifted to the court of appeals. Thus, the circuit court retained jurisdiction to entertain the petition for a writ of review in this case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals and remanded the matter for further consideration.

 

317 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harris


SC No. 26048


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 335; 599 P.2d 456; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1012


July 3, 1979, Argued and submitted  
September 11, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. * TC No. C 77-11-16147, CA No. 10825.


*  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Charles S. Crookham, Judge. 37 Or App 715, 588 P2d 100 (1978).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed a circuit court's judgment that convicted defendant of kidnapping, coercion, menacing, sexual abuse, four counts of sodomy, and illegal possession of a firearm and that imposed sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted on separate counts of the charged crimes, all arising as part of the same act and transaction, involving one 12-year-old victim during a period of several hours. The circuit court sentenced defendant to 20 years' imprisonment for each count of sodomy and five years on the sexual abuse charge, with the sentences to run concurrently and in addition to consecutive terms imposed for the other charges. Defendant, on appeal, argued that some of the counts should have been merged in the judgments of conviction and sentence. The court of appeals declined to consider the contention on the ground that it had not been adequately raised in the circuit court. The court, in reversing a part of the imposed sentence, agreed that the charge of sexual abuse should have been merged in the conviction for sodomy because the completion of the sodomy offense, under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.405, necessarily included the acts that constituted sexual abuse under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.425. The court concluded that merger did not apply to the kidnapping, menacing, and weapons charges, however, because each of the offenses could have been committed without commission of the other two.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the portion of the judgment, which imposed a separate sentence for sexual abuse, to the circuit court for entry of a corrected judgment. In all other respects, the court affirmed the judgment of the court of appeals upholding defendant's conviction and sentence.

 

318 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Mattila v. Mason


SC No. 26127


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 235; 598 P.2d 675; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1171


July 2, 1979, Argued and submitted  
August 7, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from Court of Appeals. ** TC No. 23823, CA No. 10582.


**  Appeal from Circuit Court of Columbia County, Thomas E. Edison, Judge. 38 Or App 433, 590 P2d 732 (1979).



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which dismissed his appeal from an order by the circuit court denying mandamus relief. Petitioner sought an order requiring defendant district judge to schedule a hearing as requested by petitioner on an alleged traffic infraction without requiring petitioner to first post bail.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was cited with speeding, a Class C traffic infraction. The district judge refused to schedule a hearing before petitioner posted bail. The court of appeals held that it was unable to reach the merits of the case because 1975 Or. Laws ch. 611, which made district courts courts of record and divested circuit courts of appellate review of district courts, had the effect of divesting circuit courts of authority not only to review district court actions by writ of review, but also to issue writs of mandamus to district courts. The court disagreed, finding that, upon application of the "limited jurisdiction" test, Oregon district courts, with their limited jurisdiction, were "inferior" to Oregon circuit courts, which were courts of general and unlimited jurisdiction. Thus, the circuit court had jurisdiction in the mandamus proceeding. On the merits, the court held that petitioner, by choosing to appear personally before the judge at the time and place stated in the citation, was entitled to have the matter scheduled for hearing at a date to be set at that time by the judge, regardless of whether the judge had the power to require such a driver to post bail.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals, which dismissed petitioner's appeal.

State v. Olson


SC No. 25891


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 157; 598 P.2d 670; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 984

DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which reversed an order of the trial court granting defendant's motion to quash evidence seized in his home at the time he was arrested and then subsequently charged for burglary.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the police's failure, after knocking, to announce their purpose before forcefully entering his residence was a violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 9, and of U.S. Const. amend. IV The second contention was that absent exigent circumstances, the police's entry without a warrant of arrest was a violation of the same constitutional restrictions. The appeals court held that knocking and announcing the identity of those seeking entrance was constitutionally sufficient despite the police's failure to state their purpose for seeking entry. On appeal, the court reversed for defendant holding that a warrant was necessary where there were no exigent circumstances, and in this case, there were no exigent circumstances present. The court held that because there were no exigent circumstances and the state failed to meet their burden of showing substantial probable cause existed, the police were not justified in failing to get an arrest warrant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court and reinstated the order of the circuit court quashing the evidence of the stolen goods, the admissions, and the confession.

Budget Rent-A-Car, Inc. v. Multnomah County

SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 93; 597 P.2d 1232; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1003


April 4, 1979, Argued and submitted in Portland  
July 12, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. ** TC No. A7607-09640, CA No. 8925.


**  Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Pat Dooley, Judge. 36 Or App 347, 584 P2d 767 (1978).



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' dismissal vacated; Circuit Court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which held that the corporation did not have standing to attack a tax enacted by defendant county. The trial court had previously sustained the validity of the tax.


OVERVIEW: The county enacted a tax on motor vehicle rentals. The corporation, a business engaged in the car rental business, filed an action to have the tax declared invalid. On appeal, the court vacated the appellate court's dismissal of the action and affirmed the trial court's decision that sustained the validity of the tax. The court found that the corporation did have sufficient standing to bring the action because the terms of the tax ordinance in question required the corporation to collect taxes and maintain records of taxes. The court rejected the corporation's arguments that alleged that the county did not follow the proper procedures for enacting the tax because the specific authority that the corporation cited did not apply to the tax that was at issue. The court determined that the tax was not invalid for lack of an apportionment formula because there was no proof that the tax would result in double taxation. OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's dismissal and affirmed the judgment of the trial court in sustaining the validity of the tax that was enacted by the county.

 

321 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Pike v. Allen International, Ltd.


SC No. 25568


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


287 Ore. 55; 597 P.2d 804; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 1007


March 5, 1979, Argued and submitted  
July 12, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Judgment of Circuit Court, Multnomah County. John C. Beatty, Jr., Judge. TC No. 423-775.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wine retailers appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which found that there was no justiciable controversy in the retailers' action against defendant wholesalers contending that "dock sales" by the wholesalers to members of the public violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 471.405(1), (3).


OVERVIEW: The retailers claimed that the wholesalers' dock sales of wine to the public at wholesale prices were in violation of the wholesalers' licenses under §§ 471.405(1) and (3). The trial court found that, because the Oregon Liquor Control Commission was not made a party despite being indispensable, there was no justiciable controversy. The trial court also found that the dock sales were permissible. Remanding, the court held that the commission was an indispensable party because it had the power and duty to interpret, administer and enforce the statutory provisions relating to wine sales; therefore, its interests would have been affected by any declaration made in the case. The court agreed with the retailers that the action should have been remanded with leave to join the commission as a party.


OUTCOME: The court remanded to the trial court with leave to join the commission as a party defendant and for further proceedings.

 

322 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State ex rel. Smith v. Murchison


SC Nos. 26173, 26167 (Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


286 Ore. 469; 595 P.2d 1237; 1979 Ore. LEXIS 755


May 10, 1979, Argued and submitted  
May 30, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Argued and submitted on respondent's demurrer to alternative writ May 10, 1979; Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Alternative Writ Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent circuit judge demurred to an alternative writ issued by the court. Petitioner criminal defendants brought the original proceeding in mandamus against the judge to compel him to required grand jury testimony to be recorded.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that "fundamental fairness" required that recordation be allowed because it was the only reliable means of preserving the grand jury testimony and because, in order to exercise their constitutional rights to effective assistance of counsel, confrontation of witnesses, and due process of law, such testimony had to be preserved to the extent that it might be a basis for impeachment of the testimony of witnesses at the trial. Defendants contended that recordation would not invade or detract from the secrecy of the grand jury or hamper the prosecution in presenting evidence to the grand jury and that because the prosecutor already enjoyed the right to record grand jury testimony, to deny the same privilege to defendants violated fundamental fairness and equal protection of law. The court dismissed the writ and held as follows: (1) there was no constitutional requirement that grand jury proceedings be recorded; (2) the mere failure to record grand jury testimony did not violate defendants' constitutional rights; and (3) ORS 135.855 provided that such recordings were not to be subject to discovery except for recordings of statements made by defendants.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the alternative writ of mandamus and affirmed the order of the circuit court denying defendants' motion to have the grand jury testimony recorded.

State ex rel. Drew v. Steinbock

The court ruled that it was necessary to record defendant's grand jury testimony if he testified and that all of the grand jury witnesses' testimony had to be recorded if the prosecution requested an order under Or.
