State v. Brooks


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


275 Ore. 171; 550 P.2d 440; 1976 Ore. LEXIS 780


December 3, 1975, Argued  
June 4, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from the Court of Appeals. Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Charles S. Crookham, Judge. Nos. C74-05-1335 Cr, C 74-03-0930 Cr, C 74-08-2469 Cr, C 74-11-3620 Cr, C 74-06-1918 Cr, C 74-05-1580 Cr, C 74-07-2277 Cr, C 74-07-2278 Cr, C 74-06-1981 Cr, C 74-04-1054 Cr, C 74-06-1983 Cr, C 74-08-2470 Cr.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded. Court of Appeals opinion 22 Or App 30, 537 P2d 574 (1975).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction of public indecency, a violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.465.


OVERVIEW: Defendants knowingly exposed her genitals with the intent of arousing the sexual desire of others while being seen on the stage of a theater. The theater admitted only those individuals who paid an admission fee, did not permit minors to enter the premises, and posted a warning that the entertainment included nudity. Defendants were convicted of public indecency in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.465. The conviction was affirmed by the court of appeals. On further review, the court reversed. The court held that the theater did not satisfy the statutory definition of a public place, under § 163.465. The legislature defined a public place to be a place where the public could enter at will. The legislature intentionally differentiated between a public place and places where entertainment was performed before an audience.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the court of appeals with instructions to reverse the trial court's judgment of conviction of defendants for public indecency.

Freightliner Corp. v. Department of Revenue


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


275 Ore. 13; 549 P.2d 662; 1976 Ore. LEXIS 761


March 4, 1976, Argued  
May 13, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Oregon Tax Court. Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge. Argued and submitted March 4, 1976.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Department of Revenue (Oregon) sought review of a decree from the Oregon Tax Court, which struck an omitted property assessments made by a county assessor against personal property owned by plaintiff corporate taxpayer upon finding that the assessments were invalid because they amounted to an illegal discrimination against the taxpayer and violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer's exemption for certain personal property under the Oregon Free Port Act, Or. Rev. Stat. § 307.810 et seq., was subsequently disallowed by the county assessor. The department sustained the assessor's omitted property assessments against the taxpayer for two consecutive years. On appeal, the court reversed the tax court's decision and found that the actions of the assessor and the department, in its supervisory capacity, in failing to assess back taxes in certain instances cited by the taxpayer did not demonstrate an intentional, systematic, or arbitrary discrimination against taxpayer because the taxpayer failed to carry its burden of establishing discrimination, which amounted to an intention, or the equivalent of fraudulent purpose, to disregard the fundamental principle of uniformity. Four instances in which omitted property assessments had not been levied were insufficient evidence of an intentional disregard for the principle of uniformity so as to justify relieving all other commercial taxpayers, including the taxpayer, who were properly subject to such assessments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the tax court's order striking omitted property assessments made by the assessor against the taxpayer's personal property.

State v. O'Neill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


274 Ore. 59; 545 P.2d 97; 1976 Ore. LEXIS 847


October 10, 1975, Argued  
January 22, 1976 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision from the Court of Appeals of Oregon, which affirmed a circuit court decision in which appellant was adjudicated a mentally ill person and was ordered involuntarily committed to the State Mental Health Division for treatment.


OVERVIEW: Commitment proceedings were instituted against appellant in the circuit court following an incident at his mother's home, in which she reported he "was acting up" and that she "was afraid of him." Appellant was adjudicated a mentally ill person and ordered involuntarily committed to a state hospital for treatment. Appellant sought review, challenging the sufficiency of the evidence and the constitutionality of the Oregon commitment statutes, particularly Or. Rev. Stat. § 426.005, and the appellate court affirmed. Appellant sought further review, and the court reversed, holding that there was no substantial evidence to prove that appellant was mentally ill, as defined in § 426.005, beyond a reasonable doubt, or any direct evidence that appellant was dangerous to himself or others, was unable to provide for his basic personal needs, or that he was not receiving such care as was necessary for his health or safety. The court concluded that although appellant's commitment was thus improper, the Oregon commitment statutes, and particularly Or. Rev. Stat. § 426.005, when strictly construed, were not unconstitutionally vague or an improper invasion of an individual's right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision, which had affirmed the circuit court's decision adjudicating appellant a mentally ill person and involuntarily committing him to a state hospital for treatment.

 

370 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Agri-Link Corp. v. Schmitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


272 Ore. 654; 538 P.2d 924; 1975 Ore. LEXIS 466


July 7, 1975, Argued  
August 7, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Yamhill County. Kurt C. Rossman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff instituted an action to enjoin arbitration proceedings under Or. Rev. Stat. § 607.304. The owner appealed the decision of the Circuit Court, Yamhill County (Oregon), which dismissed its complaint after the circuit court had sustained a demurrer and the owner declined to file a further amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The owner's amended complaint alleged two causes of suit: (1) that Or. Rev. Stat. § 607.304 was unconstitutional; and (2) that the arbitration proceedings under that statute were fatally defective. The court held that the question presented for decision by the pleadings at the time of this order by the trial court was not whether the statute was constitutional or unconstitutional, but whether the owner was barred by laches, waiver, or estoppel from contending that it was unconstitutional. Accordingly, the court held that the trial court should have decided the question of laches, waiver, and estoppel before undertaking to decide the constitutional question. The court concluded that the trial court also erred in sustaining the demurrer to the owner's second amended complaint and in dismissing that complaint with prejudice because the owner as the objecting party was entitled to seek the aid of a court of equity to stay the proceedings until the disqualified arbitrator had been properly replaced and to declare that any previous acts by the an arbitrator were invalid. Accordingly, the court reversed the decision of the trial court and remanded the action for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court dismissing the owner's complaint, and the court remanded the action for further proceedings.

State ex rel. Strain v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


272 Ore. 464; 537 P.2d 547; 1975 Ore. LEXIS 448


June 4, 1975, Submitted  
July 3, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ to issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a writ of mandamus to require respondent judge to allow defendant's motion for a change of judge pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 14.260.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed an original proceeding in mandamus to require the judge to allow his motion for a change of judge pursuant to § 14.260. The motion was based upon an affidavit by defendant's attorney alleging that the judge was prejudiced against defendant and his attorney. The court granted defendant's motion. The controversy between the judge and the attorney was long standing, and during it the attorney had filed approximately 26 motions and affidavits of prejudice against the judge. To support his affidavit, the attorney testified to hearsay among lawyers about the judge's bias against criminal defendants and his personal experience with the judge. An attorney who in good faith filed a motion for a change of judge in Oregon was not required to prove actual prejudice by the judge. When a judge was challenged in good faith in any given case for bias or prejudice he was not to sit in that case. An affidavit by the attorney was sufficient without an affidavit by the client. An affidavit of prejudice did not have to state specific facts. A motion for change of judge, if in good faith, could not be denied because of previous abuse.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's petition for a writ of mandamus.

 

372 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


In re Constitutionality of ORS 456.720


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


272 Ore. 398; 537 P.2d 542; 1975 Ore. LEXIS 441


June 4, 1975, Argued and submitted  
June 26, 1975 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Argued and submitted on petition of Walsh Construction Co. June 4, 1975; Original proceeding to determine constitutionality.



DISPOSITION: Constitutionality upheld.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: This was an original proceeding brought by petitioner builder, pursuant to Or. Laws ch. 97, § 2 (1975), to determine the constitutionality of provisions of the Oregon Housing Act, ORS 456.550 to 456.720, including ch. 97, § 2, and ORS 456.720, which allowed the legislature to appropriate funds to restore debt service reserves for low-income housing bonds. Respondent administrator would not issue bonds without a determination of constitutionality.


OVERVIEW: An earlier version of ORS 456.720 had been held to violate Or. Const. art. XI, § 7, insofar as the statute's mandate that the legislature restore debt service reserves for low-income housing bonds created a debt of the state in excess of $ 50,000. The legislature had amended ORS 456.720 to substitute "may" for "shall," the word that had earlier been construed as a mandate. The court held that the statute as amended did not violate the constitutional limitation on state debt because, at most, it provided for an unenforceable moral obligation on the state's part. The court also held that Or. Laws ch. 97, § 2 (1975), which authorized it to exercise original jurisdiction to issue a declaratory judgment, did not violate Or. Const. art. VII (amended), § 2. The statute contemplated resort to the court by parties who were in fact engaged in a controversy over the Housing Act's constitutionality, a judicial function regularly performed in declaratory judgment proceedings, and the case itself involved an actual controversy between a builder who was aggrieved by the administrator's refusal to issue bonds without a declaration of constitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court held that the provisions of the Housing Act that were under attack did not violate any provision of the Oregon Constitution.

DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of a decree from the Oregon Tax Court, which was in favor of defendant Department of Revenue and increased the valuation of the improvements on the taxpayer's real property for certain tax years.


OVERVIEW: The Department denied relief from assessments of the taxpayer's real property for certain tax years. The taxpayer challenged the valuation that had been placed on the improvements on her real property. The tax court entered a decree that reduced the value of the improvements, but increased the value of the land. The taxpayer contended on appeal that the tax court had no authority to increase the value of the land because she had contested only the value of the improvements. The court reversed the tax court's decree. The court concluded that the taxpayer was entitled to challenge only the value of the improvements and that the tax court was entitled to reduce the value of such improvements. The court further concluded, however, that the tax court acted improperly in adding the reduction in the improvement values to the land because the value of the land was not an issue in the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decree in favor of the Department that increased the valuation of the improvements on the taxpayer's real property.

Deras v. Myers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


272 Ore. 47; 535 P.2d 541; 1975 Ore. LEXIS 403

May 14, 1975

DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part; remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant secretary of state sought review from a Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), declaratory judgment holding Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.154 unconstitutional but finding Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.027 valid. Appellee political candidate sought review from the validation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.027 and the refusal to award attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: A political candidate filed suit seeking a declaratory judgment holding Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 260.027, 260.154 unconstitutional as improperly limiting the amounts that could be expended in support of or in opposition to candidates for public office. The secretary of state sought to enforce the statutory scheme. When the lower court invalidated Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.154 but held the other statute constitutional, both parties appealed. The court found that (1) the statutes were so intertwined that they had to stand or fall together, (2) the rights guaranteed by Or. Const. art. I, §§ 8, 26 outweighed the public interests served by the adoption of the two statutes at issue, (3) the political candidate was entitled to reasonable attorney fees because the rights he was seeking to protect were rights in which the general public had an interest, and (4) therefore the court reversed in part and affirmed in part and remanded for the award of attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that held one statute unconstitutional and reversed the portion that held a second statute constitutional and held that both statutes were unconstitutional. The court remanding the case for an appropriate attorney fees award.

Mt. Hood Radio & Television Broadcasting Corp. v. Dresser Industries, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


270 Ore. 690; 530 P.2d 72; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 338


July 8, 1974, Argued  
December 19, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Pat E. Dooley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff buyer appealed from the order of the Circuit Court of Multnomah County (Oregon) sustaining defendant manufacturer's demurrer and entering judgment for the manufacturer in the buyer's products liability action. The demurrer was sustained on the ground that the suit was time-barred under Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.115(1) and/or Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.135.


OVERVIEW: The buyer purchased a television tower that was designed and constructed by the manufacturer. When the tower fell 17 years later, the buyer brought a products liability action against the manufacturer. The manufacturer filed a demurrer alleging that the action was time barred under Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.115(1) and/or Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.135. The buyer refused to plead further, and the trial court sustained the demurrer and entered judgment for the manufacturer. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. The court noted that Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.115(1) required that a negligence action be brought within 10 years of the negligent act or omission complained of. The court also noted that Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.135 provided a 10-year limitations period for actions involving the construction of improvements on real property. The court noted that § 12.135 became effective after the tower collapsed but before the buyer filed suit. Because the court ruled that Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.115(1) applied if it was not supplanted by Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.135, the court declined to decide the latter statute's applicability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for the manufacturer in the buyer's products liability action.

School Dist. v. Wasco County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


270 Ore. 622; 529 P.2d 386; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 515


September 9, 1974, Argued  
December 12, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied January 14, 1975.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Oregon Tax Court. Carlisle B. Roberts, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, county taxing districts and taxpayers within those districts, sought review of the judgment of the Oregon Tax Court, which, in a declaratory judgment proceeding filed against defendants, other county taxing districts and county officials, upheld the constitutionality of Or. Rev. Stat. § 311.806(3).


OVERVIEW: When an aluminum plant in the county was determined to be due a refund of property taxes, plaintiffs filed suit challenging the means of implementing the refund. The trial court found in favor of defendants. Plaintiffs sought review, arguing that § 311.806(3) was unconstitutional because it violated their equal protection rights by creating a class of citizens with special privileges and immunities and because it took their property without due process. The court held that doing away with expensive and time-consuming bookkeeping and the mitigation of the shock to a few tax-levying agencies in situations where a large refund was involved were legitimate state interests, which bore a logical relation to the legislatively created classifications. Plaintiffs also claimed they were given no notice, hearing, or opportunity to object concerning the contribution of their tax money to the refund. The court noted that the state had the power to tax plaintiffs' property and that their real argument was one of discrimination because taxes were levied on them for someone else's benefit, and held that the discrimination question had already been answered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decree of the trial court upholding the constitutionality of the tax law in question and released all funds needed for disbursal.

Bryant v. Seagraves


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


270 Ore. 16; 526 P.2d 1027; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 272


July 9, 1974, Argued  
September 26, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied October 22, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Josephine County. L. L. Sawyer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the client's widower, appealed a judgment of the Circuit Court, Josephine County (Oregon), in favor of defendant law firm in a malpractice action.


OVERVIEW: The client purchased certain mineral rights from the county. Her opponent, the owner of the surface rights, notified the county that it was exercising its preferential rights under Or. Rev. Stat. § 275.298. When the county refused the opponent's tender based on Or. Rev. Stat. § 275.298(3), the opponent filed for declaratory relief. Section 275.298(3) was declared unconstitutional and the land was found unsuitable for timber development. The law firm allowed the client's appeal to lapse. The client's widower brought a malpractice action against the law firm. The judgment was in favor of the law firm. On appeal, the court reversed. Recovery was only possible if the court would have reversed the trial court's decision on appeal. The court found that it would have done so. The trial court erred in holding § 275.298(3) unconstitutional because the opponent failed to meet its burden of showing no rational basis for treating lands suitable for commercial timber production differently from lands not so suited. In addition, though the evidence was conflicting, there was sufficient evidence from which to conclude that the majority of the land was suitable for timber production.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the law firm in the malpractice action brought by the client's widower.

379 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Duerst v. Limbocker


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


269 Ore. 252; 525 P.2d 99; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 381


March 15, 1974, Submitted on briefs  
August 1, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Charles S. Crookham, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff passenger sued defendant driver for injuries received while riding in the driver's automobile. The Circuit Court of Multnomah County (Oregon) sustained the driver's demurrer to the complaint and rendered judgment for the driver. The passenger appealed.


OVERVIEW: The passenger alleged that she was riding as a guest passenger and that the driver was negligent. The driver demurred to the complaint on the ground that the Guest Passenger Statute, ORS 30.115 (Oregon), provided that no person transported without payment should have a cause of action for damages for personal injury upon ordinary negligence. On appeal, the passenger contended that her complaint should have been declared sufficient because ORS 30.115 (Oregon) was unconstitutional because the classifications created by the statute denied equal protection of the laws. The court held that the Fourteenth Amendment did not deny States the power to treat different classes of people in different ways. Rather, a classification had to be reasonable, not arbitrary, and should rest upon some ground of difference having a fair and substantial relation to the object of the legislation. Here, ORS 30.115 (Oregon) distinguished between fare-paying and gratuitous passengers for the purpose of preventing collusive lawsuits and to protect hospitality of ungrateful guests. The court held that the guest passenger statute did not operate to deny the passenger the equal protection of the laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

 

380 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Salmon v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


269 Ore. 267; 525 P.2d 104; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 384


May 6, 1974, Argued  
August 1, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Umatilla County. William W. Wells, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife of a deceased passenger, who also was injured in an automobile accident, appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Umatilla County (Oregon), which directed a verdict for defendant driver. The amended complaint alleged gross negligence on the part of the driver. The wife's motion for leave to amend to allege simple negligence was denied.


OVERVIEW: The wife's argument that the motion for leave to amend should have been granted because Or. Rev. Stat. § 30.115, Oregon's guest passenger statute, was unconstitutional was rejected. The evidence established that the husband and wife were riding in the driver's pick-up truck when it collided with another vehicle while the driver was making a left turn. The driver told a police officer at the scene of the accident that he had slowed before making the turn and used his automatic turn signal, but that he failed to see the oncoming car. The road was dry and a considerable portion of the highway was visible. The wife argued that, considering the driver's opportunity to see the traffic moving on the highway at the time he made his turn, the driver must have been more than ordinarily negligent. The court concluded that the jury could not have reasonably inferred that the driver was grossly negligent as defined by Oregon precedent. At most, the jury could have concluded that the driver failed to maintain a proper lookout. This was insufficient evidence of recklessness or gross negligence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

381 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miller


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


269 Ore. 328; 524 P.2d 1399; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 391


July 11, 1974, Argued  
August 1, 1974 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Review from Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed defendant's conviction for armed robbery and for being an ex-convict in possession of a hand gun. Defendant contended that the trial court erred when it failed to grant a hearing on his motion to suppress evidence on grounds that the motion failed to conform to Lane County, Or., Cir. Ct. R. 7.015.


OVERVIEW: The trial court allowed the State's motion to deny defendant's motion to suppress on the ground that it failed to conform to Lane County, Or., Cir. Ct. R. 7.015 in that it was unsupported by an affidavit sufficient to apprise the court and the State of the facts on which defendant relied and it alleged conclusory legal theories unsupported by facts. Defendant's convictions were affirmed, and the court granted defendant's petition for review. The court held that the trial court erred when it denied defendant's motion on the ground that the failed to satisfy the requirements of the Rule 7.015 because defendant's allegation that the search was conducted without benefit of a search warrant was sufficient to shift the burden to the State to establish the legality of the search. The court determined that the trial court's error was harmless error under Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.230 and Or. Const. art. VII, § 3, because defendant failed to object to the offer into evidence by the State of the evidence seized at the time of the warrantless search and because defendant's attorney stipulated that the evidence could be admitted into evidence without objection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions.

 

382 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Lexton-Ancira, Inc. v. Kay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


269 Ore. 1; 522 P.2d 875; 1974 Ore. LEXIS 353


May 10, 1974, Argued  
May 23, 1974 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 18, 1974.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Robert E. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, building lessee and guarantor (lessees), appealed from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) judgment for plaintiff lessor in its suit for forcible entry and detainer for possession of the leased building, and for the lessees to pay rent and real estate taxes.


OVERVIEW: The lessee leased a commercial building from the lessor. The lessee's guarantor was also the building manager. The lessee claimed error in the denial of his motion to quash out-of-state service on him because he had only four days to prepare for trial and the summons did not indicate he had to answer in that time, thus failing to give adequate notice for jurisdiction, and the time allowed him as a nonresident was so unreasonable as to be a deprivation of due process. The guarantor argued it was error to enter judgment against him because there was a failure of proof regarding non-payment of rent. The court affirmed. This was not a case of an indigent, incompetent, underprivileged, or unrepresented tenant. Tenants were expected to know the terms of their lease, whether they paid their rent, were in possession of the premises, or had received a proper notice to quit. There was no failure of proof as to nonpayment, but rather substantial evidence to support the findings and decision that the obligation had not been met and the lessor was entitled to immediate possession. Nor was there any evidence of "retaliatory eviction" or any other improper purpose.


OUTCOME: Finding no error, the judgment granting the lessor immediate possession of the leased building against the lessees was affirmed. The summons was sufficient to confer jurisdiction over the nonresident lessee. The lessee was not denied due process. There was no error in entering judgment against the guarantor, whose plea in abatement was not well taken.

W. J. Seufert Land Co. v. National Restaurant Supply Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


266 Ore. 92; 511 P.2d 363; 1973 Ore. LEXIS 336; 1973-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P74,643


May 7, 1973, Argued  
June 21, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Wasco County. J. R. Campbell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff buyer appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Wasco County (Oregon), dismissing complaint alleging defendant seller's violation of the Oregon Anti-price Discrimination Law, Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 646.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff buyer sued defendant seller for violating the Oregon Anti-price Discrimination Law, Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 646. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's complaint on the basis that Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 646 applied only to intrastate commerce. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the transaction was subject to Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 646, since the goods originated in, were delivered in, and were negotiated in Oregon so as to constitute commerce within the state, even though defendant was a Washington corporation and some goods crossed state lines. The statute was not preempted by the Robinson-Patman Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 13 (c). Defendant's payment of ten percent of purchase price received from plaintiff to restaurant lessee that had arranged to subsequently buy restaurant equipment from plaintiff could reasonably lead a trier of fact to find that defendant discriminated in the price charged to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to allow plaintiff an opportunity to amend complaint to allege a discriminatory price scheme, since the trial court erred in dismissing plaintiff's complaint.

On review, the court affirmed, holding that the requirement was rationally based to a legitimate legislative objective and did not violate the injured party's due process or equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the injured party's complaint against the driver.

Ward v. Oregon State Board of Nursing


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


266 Ore. 128; 510 P.2d 554; 1973 Ore. LEXIS 339; 55 A.L.R.3d 1134


March 5, 1973, Argued  
June 1, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied June 26, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Oregon State Board of Nursing (board) sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed the judgment of a circuit court that set aside an order of the board that revoked the nursing license of respondent nurse. The Court of Appeals held that there was insufficient evidence before the board to establish what activities constituted the duties of a registered nurse in Oregon.


OVERVIEW: The nurse permitted and instructed her daughter to perform and serve as a registered nurse and recommended her as a registered nurse. On appeal, the court held that the Court of Appeals had confused the issues of (1) what evidence was necessary to establish a standard of professional conduct, and (2) what evidence was sufficient to prove that a standard, once established, was breached. The court noted that Or. Rev. Stat. § 678.021(2) provided that it was unlawful for an unlicensed person to hold oneself out to be a licensed professional nurse and that Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.220 provided that it was a crime to aid or abet in any criminal act. The court held that the correct standard of judicial review by the circuit court was in former Or. Rev. Stat. § 678.121(3), which allowed the circuit court to hear additional evidence and to make supplemental findings of fact only as to such additional evidence, but which did not allow a de novo review. The court held that the circuit court should have accorded proper deference to the findings made by the board and used the substantial evidence standard of review. The court found such evidence was clear from the record.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the Court of Appeals that had affirmed the reversal of the board's order of revocation.

 

386 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. United States Nat'l Bank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


265 Ore. 234; 509 P.2d 442; 1973 Ore. LEXIS 427; 12 U.C.C. Rep. Serv. (Callaghan) 549


November 14, 1972, Submitted on briefs  
April 19, 1973 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Edwin E. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant bank appealed a decision from the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which found it liable for trespass and conversion of a used car that it had repossessed from plaintiff owners.


OVERVIEW: The owners bought a car on an installment sale contract that the seller sold to the bank. The contract allowed the bank to repossess the car in the event of default or to elect any other legal or equitable remedy. When the owners failed to make payments on the car, the bank notified them of their default and its intent to repossess. The owners said they would surrender the car peaceably if the bank got a court order. The bank then repossessed the car without breach of the peace. The owners sued the bank, alleging that Or. Rev. Stat. § 79.5030, allowing the bank to use self-help, was unconstitutional. The trial court found the bank liable for trespass and conversion. On appeal, the court held that: (1) procedural due process required an opportunity for a hearing before the state authorized its agents to take property, (2) the right to procedural due process did not attach to deprivation of property by private actors, and (3) the enactment of § 79.5030, allowing private self-help repossessions, did not amount to sufficient state involvement in otherwise private acts to constitute state action necessary to invoke due process protections.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision finding the bank liable to the owners for conversion and trespass.

 

387 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Campbell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


265 Ore. 82; 506 P.2d 163; 1973 Ore. LEXIS 409


November 2, 1972, Argued  
February 15, 1973 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied March 30, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a canvasser and sponsors, sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which in a consolidated appeal, affirmed their convictions for paying or receiving money for securing signatures of electors, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 254.590.


OVERVIEW: The sponsors initiated a referendum campaign. They hired a paid canvasser to conduct a petition drive. The canvasser paid circulators to obtain petition signatures. Thereafter, the trial court found defendants guilty of violating § 254.590. The court of appeals affirmed the convictions, holding that § 254.590 prohibited compensation as to persons who circulated petitions and those who organized and coordinated a referendum. On appeal, the court held that § 254.590 was a reasonable regulation that facilitated the proper exercise of the initiative and referendum. Further, § 254.590 did not place too great a burden on the legitimate exercise of the initiative and referendum powers by the people. Thus, insofar as § 254.590 prohibited the use of paid canvassers to personally solicit signatures, it was a valid regulation permitted by Or. Const. art. VI, § 1. Moreover, contrary to the court of appeals' interpretation, § 254.590 prohibited the payment of compensation only to those who personally solicited or secured signatures on petitions. However, there was sufficient evidence to support the trial court's finding that the sponsors violated § 254.590 through the acts of the canvasser.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for paying or receiving money for securing signatures of electors.

 

388 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Valentine


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


264 Ore. 54; 504 P.2d 84; 1972 Ore. LEXIS 343


May 4, 1972, Argued on review  
December 14, 1972 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Stay of Mandate Allowed by Supreme Court January 3, 1973.


PRIOR HISTORY: On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court denied defendants' motion to suppress the introduction of narcotics seized by the authorities. The defendants were convicted of the possession of narcotics. The Court of Appeals (Oregon) affirmed. The court granted the petition for review. The issue was whether evidence should have been suppressed because it had been obtained by officers entering the premises without first knocking and announcing their presence and purpose.


OVERVIEW: A federal undercover agent purchased hashish from one of the defendants. The agent negotiated to buy a large quantity of hashish. The agent was told to come to defendants' apartment to make the purchase. A search warrant was obtained and delivered to a police officer. The agent was admitted to the apartment and was shown a five-pound box of hashish. He left the apartment, ostensibly to get the money, and left the slightly open. The agent gave the signal to go in and arrest. One of the other officers, in plain clothes, stepped inside with a drawn gun and announced that he was a police officer. The other officers entered. The hashish was found. The State did not contend that the officers knocked or announced their presence before they entered. The trial court denied the motion to suppress and convicted defendants. The appellate court affirmed. An otherwise lawful search and seizure accomplished by an entry that was made without an announcement of presence and purpose was not an unreasonable search and seizure within the meaning of Or. Const. art. I, § 9. A trial was a search for the truth and competent evidence was never excluded except for the most compelling reason.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

McAlmond v. Myers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


262 Ore. 521; 500 P.2d 457; 1972 Ore. LEXIS 505


August 4, 1972, Argued and submitted  
August 16, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Argued and submitted on respondent's demurrer to alternative writ of mandamus August 4, 1972; Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Demurrer overruled; peremptory writ issued.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Oregon Secretary of State, filed a demurrer to the issue of an alternative writ of mandamus by petitioner unsuccessful candidate that sought to compel the Secretary to remove the name of intervenor successful candidate from the list of nominees certified for election for violation of the Corrupt Practices Act, Or. Rev. Stat. ch. 260.


OVERVIEW: The unsuccessful candidate filed a petition for a writ of mandamus seeking to remove the successful candidate from the ballot of qualified nominees for Oregon State Treasurer because of her violation of an election law in a prior election. The court granted the alternative writ, and the Secretary filed a demurrer. The court overruled the demurrer and made the writ peremptory. The court determined that the Secretary had a duty to remove her name pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 249.031(2)(f), 249.221(1)(f) because the successful candidate was disqualified under Or. Rev. Stat. § 260.365 where her period of disability following the prior election would not have ended prior to the day that she would take office under the current election. The court further determined that while the unsuccessful candidate did not avail himself of the remedy of a circuit court petition, such failure did not prevent the issue of the writ because the right to be vindicated was a public as well as a private one. The court concluded that the present remedy of a written complaint to the Secretary was not an adequate remedy preventing the issue of the writ.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the Secretary's demurrer and made the writ peremptory.

Ortwein v. Schwab


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


262 Ore. 375; 498 P.2d 757; 1972 Ore. LEXIS 488


January 31, 1972, Submitted on petition  
June 22, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Filing of petition denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner welfare recipients filed an application for a writ of mandamus to compel respondent Oregon Court of Appeals to accept a petition for review of administrative orders without the payment of the filing fee.


OVERVIEW: The welfare recipients sought judicial review of decisions of the Oregon State Welfare Division, which reduced their welfare payments. They contended that the statutes requiring filing fees of indigents were invalid because they were contrary to the First and Fourteenth Amendments and Or. Const. art. I, § 10. They argued that that the requirement of the payment of a filing fee deprived them of their rights under the Equal Protection Clause and Due Process. The court denied the petition for a writ of mandamus. The court held that the right to obtain judicial review of a ruling the Welfare Division was not a fundamental personal right. The court found that the First Amendment was not relevant to the instant case. The court noted that due process did not demand judicial review of administrative decisions. The court concluded that the failure to provide for judicial review of the order reducing the welfare recipients' payments was not violative of the U.S. or Oregon Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied the welfare recipients' petition for a writ of mandamus.

Buchea v. Sullivan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


262 Ore. 222; 497 P.2d 1169; 1972 Ore. LEXIS 474


December 6, 1971, Argued on review  
June 1, 1972 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for review allowed November 3, 1971; On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Sentence set aside, case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which denied defendant's petition for post-conviction relief concerning the trial court's denial of his request to review a presentence investigation report.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to attempted burglary in a dwelling. The trial judge ordered a presentence investigation report to be prepared for his consideration which defendant, through his attorney, requested permission to see prior to his being sentenced. The trial court denied the request and sentenced defendant to the maximum sentence authorized. On appeal, the court reversed and held that it was error not to furnish defendant with a copy of that part of the presentence report that related to his prior criminal record. The court held that defendant, as a person convicted of a crime, had a right under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments and Or. Const. art. I, §11 to see the presentence report considered by a judge in his determination of sentence. However, defendant would only be allowed to point out factual matters in the report which he considered to be untrue or misleading, leaving it to the trial judge to ascertain the truth or falsity by such means as he deemed proper, or if he chose, to remove the challenged matter from his consideration. The court set aside the imposed sentence and remanded for a new sentencing hearing.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the sentence imposed upon defendant and remanded the cause to the trial court for resentencing.

 

392 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Josephs v. Burns


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


260 Ore. 493; 491 P.2d 203; 1971 Ore. LEXIS 332


October 11, 1971, Argued  
December 1, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. William M. Dale, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, the owners, lessors, and lessees, appeal the judgments entered by the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which sustained demurrers upon the basis that the statute of limitations imposed by Or. Rev. Stat. § 12.115(1) had run prior to the commencement of plaintiffs' complaints against defendants, architects and engineers, for damages after the collapse of a roof that allegedly resulted from defendants' negligence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought a negligence action for damages to real and personal property, and for the resulting loss of income, after the collapse of a roof that allegedly resulted from defendants' negligence in supervision and construction. However, the roof collapse occurred approximately 17 years after the services were rendered, and the trial court sustained demurrers. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the 10-year time limit prescribed in § 12.115(1) was intended as an over-all limit regardless of circumstances to begin from the date of the act or omission. The court found that it was a proper function of the legislature to limit the availability of causes of action by the use of statutes of limitation so long as it is done for the purpose of protecting a recognized public interest. The court ruled that it was in the interest of the public that there was a definite end to the possibility of future litigation resulting from past actions, and that it was a permissible constitutional legislative function to balance the possibility of outlawing legitimate claims against the public need that at some definite time there would have been an end to potential litigation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of defendants in plaintiffs' complaints seeking damages after the collapse of a roof that allegedly resulted from defendants' negligence.

 

393 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Hovet v. Myers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


260 Ore. 152; 489 P.2d 684; 1971 Ore. LEXIS 292


September 8, 1971, Argued  
September 30, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for review.



DISPOSITION: Petition granted. Plan returned to Secretary of State with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners filed an action requesting the court review a plan for the reapportionment of the state legislature submitted by the Secretary of State which violated the apportionment provisions of Or. Const. art. IV, § 6(1) and § 7.


OVERVIEW: Oregon's Constitution was amended to require the legislature to reapportion districts following the federal census. If it failed to do so, the Secretary of State was required to do so. When the legislature failed to reapportion after the 1970 census, the Secretary of State submitted a plan which created single-member districts. Petitioners challenged the action, asserting that the Secretary had acted in violation of the apportionment provisions of Or. Const. art IV, § 6 (1) and § 7, which required the districts be apportioned by determining the ideal population for each district by dividing the total population of Oregon by the constitutionally mandated 30 senators and 60 representatives. The Secretary argued that it was impossible to apportion the districts that way because it resulted in some districts being overrepresented and others under-represented as the constitution also required the districts to follow county lines. The court held that to satisfy Equal Protection, the Secretary could create single-member districts in violation of the Oregon Constitution. However, the Secretary exceeded his authority when he created districts to affect residency.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petition for review and returned the plan to the Secretary with instructions to correct the reapportionment as it related to the districts created to affect residency.

 

394 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. McMaster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


259 Ore. 291; 486 P.2d 567; 1971 Ore. LEXIS 377


April 5, 1971, Argued  
June 23, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On review from the Court of Appeals.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parents sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Oregon), which affirmed an order terminating the parents' rights in their child. The parents claimed that the lower court erred in overruling their demurrer to the petition to terminate their rights on the grounds that the controlling statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.523 (2) (a), was a violation of due process of law and also was unconstitutionally broad, uncertain, and vague.


OVERVIEW: Parents whose rights to their child had been terminated by the lower court sought review of that decision. On review, the parents claimed that the controlling statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 419.523 (2)(a), was unconstitutional as a violation of due process of law under the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments of the Constitution of the United States and unconstitutionally broad, uncertain, and vague under Or. Const. art. I, § 10. The court found that in juvenile court, the proceedings might be more flexible than in a criminal court, but the rights of the parties were not without constitutional protection. The court concluded that even though the statute in question did not specify the exact behavior that would lead to termination of parental rights, the legislature intended that this be conduct detrimental to any major aspect of the child. The court did not find the statute to be unconstitutionally vague. Even so, the court reversed the lower court's decision to terminate parental rights. The court noted the numerous shortcomings of the parents but concluded that their poverty and instability were not seriously detrimental to their child as intended by the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the lower court that ordered the parental rights of the petitioners to be terminated.

 

395 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Leech v. Georgia-Pacific Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


259 Ore. 161; 485 P.2d 1195; 1971 Ore. LEXIS 365


February 5, 1971, Argued  
June 16, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Coos County. John C. Warden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a mentally handicapped adult daughter, challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court, Coos County (Oregon), which sustained respondent employer's demurrer on the ground that it failed to state a cause of action. The daughter filed a negligence action against the employer following the death of her father. Her mother received workmen's compensation death benefits that did not include an additional amount for the dependent daughter.


OVERVIEW: At the time of the workman's death there was no provision in the workmen's compensation statute to include mentally retarded adult children as beneficiaries entitled to a benefit. The man's adult daughter was totally dependent on her parents and unable to provide for herself. She filed a negligence action against her father's employer. On appeal of the judgment entered against her, the court noted that the former Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 656.204 and 002 made no provision for additional benefits on behalf of a mentally handicapped child. Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.002(4) was amended to provide that such an adult child was to be considered as a child under the age of 18 for the computation of benefits. The revision did not apply to the daughter. The court rejected her argument that a person not considered to be a beneficiary had the right to file a negligence action against a decedent's employer. Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.018 provided the exclusive remedy available to an employee, his beneficiaries, or anyone else otherwise entitled to recover damages from an employer for the employee's injuries or death. It rejected the daughter's claim that the statute violated her right to equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State ex rel Mullican v. Parsons


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


257 Ore. 468; 479 P.2d 734; 1971 Ore. LEXIS 490


January 5, 1971, Argued  
January 27, 1971 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lincoln County. Stewart Weiss, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Lincoln County, Oregon, which was entered in his quo warranto proceeding challenging the right of defendant judge to hold and exercise the functions of the office of municipal judge of a city. The trial court sustained a demurrer to plaintiff's complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's complaint alleged that he was charged with an offense before the municipal court of the city. He contended that the judge was subject to requirements of the Oregon Constitution, Art VII (Amended), § 1, which provided that judges of the supreme and other courts were to be elected by the legal voters of the state or of their respective districts. Plaintiff argued that the judge was appointed by the council of the city, apparently pursuant to ORS 221.140, and that ORS 221.140 was unconstitutional and void as in conflict with the Oregon Constitution, Art VII (Amended), § 1. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment. The court ruled that the constitutional requirement that the judges of the supreme and other courts were to be elected was not intended to include judges of municipal courts of cities. Municipal voters had power to provide by a municipal charter that municipal judges were to be appointed by the city council.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

397 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State Forester v. Umpqua River Navigation Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


258 Ore. 10; 478 P.2d 631; 1970 Ore. LEXIS 449


July 14, 1970, Argued  
December 23, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied February 23, 1971.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Douglas County. Don H. Sanders, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, the Oregon state forester, appealed a judgment from the Douglas County Circuit Court (Oregon) in a negligence action brought under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 477.064--477.085 to recover the fire suppression costs incurred in fighting a large forest fire. A jury returned a verdict for defendant road construction company.


OVERVIEW: Fire investigators, suspecting that the fire was caused by the company's roller emitting sparks, operated and dismantled the roller without the knowledge or consent of the company. The forester brought an action alleging negligence. The company claimed the forester's inspection prevented the company from subsequently conducting relevant tests to demonstrate its freedom from negligence. The trial court granted the company's motion to suppress upon the ground that the search was unconstitutional. After a jury verdict in favor of the company, the forester appealed. On appeal, the Oregon Supreme Court reversed. The court found there was no known need to deter unconstitutional conduct of state employees searching for the cause of a fire, as there was to deter law enforcement officers investigating suspected criminal activity. The court also reasoned that effective remedies, other than exclusion of competent evidence were available. The court held that illegally seized evidence should only be excluded in a criminal or quasi-criminal proceeding and if it was obtained by law enforcement officers investigating criminal activity.


OUTCOME: The judge reversed the judgment that suppressed evidence obtained from the fire investigators' search of the construction company's roller. The court held that the trial court was correct in finding against the company on its affirmative defense of contributory negligence. The court remanded.

 

398 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Nielson v. Bryson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


257 Ore. 179; 477 P.2d 714; 1970 Ore. LEXIS 263


November 2, 1970, Argued  
December 9, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Peremptory writ allowed in part; disallowed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner personal injury plaintiff brought a mandamus action against respondent circuit court judge to set aside the judge's order that required the medical records custodian of the physician and hospital that provided medical treatment to the plaintiff to produce the plaintiff's medical records. Plaintiff contended that Or. Rev. Stat. § 44.040 (1)(d) precluded the disclosure of such medical records and that § 441.510 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The plaintiff was a personal injury claimant in an underlying action. The judge issued an order that directed the medical records custodian of the plaintiff's physician to produce the medical records pertaining to the plaintiff's treatment and also ordered such records produced from the hospital where the plaintiff was treated. The plaintiff sought a writ of mandamus to set aside the order and the court granted the writ pertaining to the physician's records, finding that the physician-patient privilege established in § 44.040 (1)(d) applied to the physician and his records and precluded the disclosure of those records until waived by the plaintiff offering himself as a witness in the action pursuant to § 44.040 (2). The court denied the writ as to the hospital records, holding that § 441.510 permitted discovery of those records and that the statute was not unconstitutional because the original legislative act in which it was included did not violate the one subject rule of Or. Const. art IV, § 20. The court concluded that the provisions of § 441.510 pertained to the general purpose of the original legislative act in which it appeared.


OUTCOME: The writ of mandamus was granted as to the medical records of the plaintiff's physician but denied as to the medical records of the hospital.

 

399 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Henkel v. Bradshaw


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


257 Ore. 55; 475 P.2d 75; 1970 Ore. LEXIS 246


May 8, 1970, Argued  
October 7, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Rehearing Denied November 10, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Original proceeding in mandamus.



DISPOSITION: Demurrer sustained; peremptory writ disallowed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father requested the court to issue a writ of mandamus ordering respondent judge to grant the father's motion to appoint counsel to represent him at no personal expense in a contempt proceeding.


OVERVIEW: The father was ordered to appear before the judge to show cause why he should not have been held in contempt for failure to make court-ordered child support payments. In response to the bench warrant for arrest, the father filed a motion with the judge to determine his financial status and, if he were found to have been indigent, to appoint counsel to represent him at no personal expense. The judge denied the motion, and the father requested the court to issue a writ of mandamus requiring the judge to grant the motion. In disallowing the writ, the court held that the father had a speedy and adequate remedy available to him in the form of an appeal. Thus, it ruled that mandamus was not necessary.


OUTCOME: The court disallowed the writ.

 

400 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Oregon AFL-CIO v. Weldon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


SUPREME COURT OF OREGON


256 Ore. 307; 473 P.2d 664; 1970 Ore. LEXIS 324


July 27, 1970, Submitted on briefs  
August 17, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Ballot Title certified by George A. Van Hoomissen, District Attorney, Multnomah County.



DISPOSITION: Ballot Title revised.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Tri-County Metropolitan Transportation District (MTD) sought review of a ballot title prepared and certified by respondent county district attorney for a proposed initiative ordinance repealing an excise tax on employers levied by the board of directors of the MTD and restricting the board's taxing powers.


OVERVIEW: The district attorney certified the ballot title for a proposed initiative ordinance repealing an excise tax on employers levied by the board of directors of the MTD, a municipal corporation, and restricting the board's taxing powers. The MTD objected to the ballot title, claiming that a number of the measure's provisions were invalid. The MTD sought judicial review of the ballot title. The MTD also claimed that the ballot title was misleading in its emphasis and did not disclose all the effects the measure would have if enacted. The court held that it was without power to determine the validity of the proposed ordinance before its enactment. It would have been improper for the district attorney to refuse to give the measure a title merely because he thought all or portions of it would be invalid if enacted. Nor would it have been proper for him or the court to decide in advance the validity of the measure and incorporate that decision in the ballot title. The court revised the ballot title to reflect that the primary purpose of the proposed ordinance was the repeal of the existing payroll tax and to mention the restriction on future collections of taxes already levied.


OUTCOME: The court revised the ballot title prepared and certified by the district attorney to remedy certain defects and approved the ballot title as modified.
